Six Categories of Possible Opposites and Divine Will
Author enumerates six categories of mutually exclusive possibilities and shows how will selects one of each pair.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the six categories of mutually exclusive possibilities in creation and how Allah's will selects one from each pair.

Core Idea
Divine will chooses among six kinds of opposites, not just existence and non-existence.

The author now deepens our understanding of divine will by enumerating the types of contingent things that are subject to choice. He shows that will is not limited to bringing something into existence; it also determines every quality, time, place, direction, and measure. This lesson will walk through each category and how will applies.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once through, then read the translation. Pay attention to the structure: the author first lists the six categories, then explains 'opposites,' and finally applies will to each.
Notice the poetic couplet cited by the author.
2
Identify the six categories
From the passage, list the six categories of opposites: existence/non-existence, attributes, times, places, directions, and measures. For each, note that the opposites are mutually exclusive.
The categories are given in the couplet and then expanded.
3
See how will attaches to each
The passage concludes by showing that divine will specifies one side of each pair to occur. For example, it can choose existence over non-existence, or a particular attribute over another. Reflect on the comprehensive scope of divine will.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
واعلم أن الممكنات المتقابلات ستة ، وقد أشار إليها بعضهم في قوله: الممكناتُ المُتَقَابِلَاتُ وُجُودُنَا وَالْعَدَمُ الصِّفَاتُ أَزْمَنَةٌ ، أَمْكِنَةٌ ، جِهَاتٌ كَذَا المَقَادِيرُ ، رَوَى الثِّقَاتُ ومعنى كونها متقابلات أنها متنافياتٌ ؛ فالوجود يقابل العدم، وبالعكس، وبعض الصفات يقابل بعضاً، فكونه أبيض مثلا يقابل كونَه أسودَ ، وبعض الأزمنة يقابل بعضا، فكونه في زمن الطوفان مثلا يقابل كونه في زمن سيّدنا محمد الله ، وبعض الأمكنة يقابل بعضاً، فكونه في مصر يقابل كونه في تونس مثلاً ، وبعض الجهات يقابل بعضاً، فكونه في جهة المشرق يقابل كونَه في جهة المغرب مثلاً ، وبعض المقادير يقابل بعضا، فكونه طويلا مثلا يقابل كونه قصيراً . الممكنات فقول النَّاظم في البيتين اللذين رويناهما لك: «وجودنا والعدم»، واحد من المتقابلات، وقوله: (والصفات»، واحدٌ ثانٍ ، والثَّالث: الأمكنة، والرابع: الأزمنة، والخامس الجهات، والسادس: المقادير . فالإرادة تخصص الوجود الذي هو أحد الطرفين بالوقوع دون العدم، أو تخصص العدمَ الذي هو الطرف الآخر بالوقوع دون الوجود، وتخصص الصفة المخصوصة كالبياض مثلا بالوقوع دون غيرها من الصفات، وتخصص الزمان المخصوص بالوقوع فيه دون غيره من الأزمنة ، وتخصص المكان المخصوص بالوقوع فيه دون غيره من الأمكنة، وتخصص الجهة المخصوصة بالوقوع فيها دون غيرها من الجهات، وتخصص المقدار المخصوص بالوقوع للجرم دون غيره من المقادير .
And know that the possible things that are mutually opposite are six. Some have alluded to them in his saying: 'The possible opposites are: our existence and non-existence, attributes, times, places, directions, likewise measures, the trustworthy have narrated.' And the meaning of them being opposites is that they are mutually exclusive: existence contrasts with non-existence, and vice versa. And some attributes contrast with some: for example, its being white contrasts with its being black. And some times contrast with some: for example, its being at the time of the Flood contrasts with its being at the time of our master Muhammad, may Allah bless him and grant him peace. And some places contrast with some: for example, its being in Egypt contrasts with its being in Tunis, for example. And some directions contrast with some: for example, its being in the direction of the east contrasts with its being in the direction of the west, for example. And some measures contrast with some: for example, its being long contrasts with its being short. So the saying of the versifier in the two lines that we have narrated to you: 'our existence and non-existence' is one of the opposites, and his saying: 'and attributes' is a second, and the third: places, and the fourth: times, and the fifth: directions, and the sixth: measures. So the will specifies existence, which is one of the two sides, to occur rather than non-existence; or it specifies non-existence, which is the other side, to occur rather than existence; and it specifies the particular attribute, like whiteness for example, to occur rather than other attributes; and it specifies the particular time to occur in it rather than other times; and it specifies the particular place to occur in it rather than other places; and it specifies the particular direction to occur in it rather than other directions; and it specifies the particular measure to occur for the body rather than other measures.
Source pages p. 240

Commentary
This passage classifies possible things into six categories of opposites and explains how divine will attaches to each. The author first states that there are six mutually exclusive (mutaqabilat) pairs: existence/non-existence, attributes (e.g., white/black), times (e.g., Flood era vs. Prophet's era), places (e.g., Egypt vs. Tunis), directions (e.g., east vs. west), and measures (e.g., long vs. short). He cites a poetic couplet that lists them. He then explains that 'opposites' means they cannot coexist. Finally, he shows that the divine will selects one side from each pair to occur: it can choose existence over non-existence, a particular color over others, a specific time, place, direction, or measure. This demonstrates the comprehensive scope of God's will over all contingent determinations.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think the divine will only concerns existence vs. non-existence.
Correction: The passage shows that will also determines attributes, times, places, directions, and measures — all categories of contingent opposites.

Checkpoint
How many categories of possible opposites does the author enumerate?
A) Four
B) Five
C) Six
D) Seven

Key Takeaway
Allah's will encompasses every contingent determination, from existence to measure, selecting one from each pair of opposites.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Your existence, every attribute you have, the time you live in, the place, your direction in life, your measure – all were chosen by Allah's will. Does this fill you with awe and gratitude, or do you sometimes resent His choices for you?
?
Which category of opposites (existence, attributes, time, place, direction, measure) do you struggle most to accept from Allah's will? Will you today surrender that specific area to Him, trusting His wisdom over your preference?
?
If Allah's will specifies every detail, then every aspect of your life is intentional. How does this change the way you view the challenges you face? Are you willing to see them as part of a divine plan rather than random accidents?

The Proof for the Necessity of Divine Will
Sanusi presents a conditional syllogism proving divine will.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the syllogistic proof that establishes the necessity of God having the attribute of will, based on the existence of the world.

Core Idea
The existence of the world proves God must will it; otherwise He would be coerced.

This lesson presents a concise but powerful proof from classical Islamic theology. The argument is straightforward: if God did not possess will, He would be forced to act—yet the world exists with order and particularity, which cannot emerge from a coerced agent. This proof mirrors an earlier argument for God's power, and we will examine its logical structure step by step.

Guided Path
1
Read the proof carefully
Read the Arabic passage and then the English translation. Notice how the author uses conditional statements.
2
Follow the logical chain
Identify the four key propositions: the condition (if no will), the intermediate (then coercion), the consequence (then no world), and the observed fact (world exists). See how they lead to the conclusion.
3
Reflect on the analogy
The author says this proof is like the earlier proof for power. Consider why the same syllogistic form can apply to different attributes.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما الدليل على وجوب اتصاف الله تعالى بالإرادة فتقريره أن نقول: لو لم تجب له الإرادة لكان مُكرَها ، ولو كان مكرها لما وجد شيءٌ مِن هذا العالم، ولكن العالم موجود، فثبت كونه سبحانه متصفا بالإرادة ، والكلام على هذا الدليل كالكلام على دليل القدرة ، وقد تقدم قريباً.
As for the proof for the necessity of Allah, may He be exalted, being described with will, its formulation is that we say: if will were not necessary for Him, then He would be forced; and if He were forced, then nothing of this world would exist; but the world does exist, so it is established that He, may He be exalted, is described with will. And the discussion on this proof is like the discussion on the proof for power (qudra), and it has preceded recently.
Source pages p. 241

Commentary
This passage provides a proof (dalil) for the necessity of God having the attribute of will. The proof is a conditional syllogism: (1) If God did not possess will, He would be coerced (mukrah). (2) If He were coerced, nothing in this world would exist. (3) But the world does exist. (4) Therefore, God must possess will. The reasoning is that a coerced agent cannot produce a world with order, purpose, and specific determinations; the very existence of contingent things—each with particular attributes—implies an act of will that selects among possibilities. The author notes that this argument is analogous to the previously given proof for the attribute of power (qudra), implying a similar structure. The proof is concise but foundational: it grounds the attribute of will in the observable reality of the world's existence.

Key Terms
دليل
وجوب اتصاف
مكره
عالم موجود
قدرة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The proof is circular because it assumes the world exists.
Correction: The proof does not assume any conclusion about God; it starts from the observable fact of the world's existence and deduces will as the only plausible explanation. There is no circularity because the premises are independent.

Checkpoint
What is the logical conclusion if God were coerced (mukrah), according to the proof?
A) The world would be perfect
B) The world would not exist
C) God would be unjust
D) Will would be unnecessary

Key Takeaway
The existence of the world logically necessitates that God possesses will, because a coerced agent could not produce the world's intricate order.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If God's will is necessary, then every moment of your life is deliberately chosen by Him, not left to chance. How does that change the way you view the difficulties you are facing right now? Will you submit to His will or resent it?
?
Sanusi's proof begins with the existence of the world. When you look at your own existence—your strengths, weaknesses, and circumstances—do you see them as evidence of a Willing God, or as random accidents? What would it mean for you to truly live as someone who believes God willed every detail of your being?
?
The proof says without will, God would be 'mukrah'—forced. Reflect: Do you sometimes feel forced in your obedience to God? How can you transform that feeling into one of free, joyful surrender, knowing that His will for you is perfect wisdom and mercy?

The Two Types of Attachment of Divine Will
Author explains the two main attachments of will: pre-eternal determinative and pre-eternal potential, and critiques a third view.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the two primary attachments of divine will (determinative and potential) and why a third temporal attachment is not independent.

Core Idea
The divine will has two eternal attachments, not three.

The author explains how the divine will relates to creation. Scholars traditionally identify two attachments: one determinative and one potential. Some proposed a third temporal attachment, but the author argues it is merely a manifestation of the first. This distinction preserves the immutability of God's will.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before interpreting.
Notice the technical terms 'ta'liq tanjizi' and 'ta'liq suluhi'.
2
Identify the two main attachments
Find the descriptions of the pre-eternal determinative attachment and the pre-eternal potential attachment. Note how each is defined.
The first is about God's eternal specification; the second is about the will's inherent capability.
3
Understand the critique of the third attachment
Read the author's refutation of the third attachment. Recognize that what scholars call 'originated determinative attachment' is actually just the manifestation of the first.
The key phrase is 'this is a manifestation of the pre-eternal determinative attachment'.
4
Reflect on the theological implication
Think about why this matters: God's will does not change or acquire new decisions in time. The temporal act only executes the eternal decree.
This preserves divine immutability and simplicity.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما بيان تعلق الإرادة فاعلم أولا: أنَّ الذي ذكره المحققون من تعلقات الإرادة تعلقين، الأَوَّل: التَّعلق التنجيزي القديم، وهو: تخصيص الله تعالى الشيء أزلا بالصفات التي يعلمُ أَنَّه يوجَدُ عليها في الخارج وقد سبق بيان هذا، والثاني: تعلق صلوحي قديم، وهو: صلاحية الإرادة في الأزل للتخصيص مع ثبوت التخصيص بالفعل أزلا أيضا» ومن العلماء من أثبت للإرادة تعلقاً ثالثًا، وسماه «التعلق التنجيزي الحادث» وهو: «تخصيص الله تعالى الشيء عند إيجاده بالفعل بما سبق تعلقها به «أزلا والتحقيق أنَّ هذا إظهار للتعلق التنجيزي القديم، وليس تعلقا مُستقلا
As for the explanation of the attachment (ta'liq) of will, know, firstly, that which the verifiers (al-muhaqqiqun) have mentioned regarding the attachments of will: two attachments. The first: the pre-eternal determinative attachment (al-ta'liq al-tanjizi al-qadim), which is: Allah's specification of a thing pre-eternally with the attributes upon which He knows it will exist externally, and the explanation of this has preceded. The second: a pre-eternal potential attachment (ta'liq suluhi qadim), which is: the capability of will pre-eternally for specification along with the actual specification being established also pre-eternally. And among scholars are those who affirmed for will a third attachment, and called it 'the originated determinative attachment' (al-ta'liq al-tanjizi al-hadith), which is: 'Allah's specification of a thing at the time of its actual bringing into existence with that to which its attachment had preceded pre-eternally.' And the investigation is that this is a manifestation of the pre-eternal determinative attachment, and it is not an independent attachment.
Source pages p. 241

Commentary
This passage explains the different ways (ta'liqat) in which the divine will relates to creation. The verifiers (muhaqqiqun) identify two attachments. The first is the pre-eternal determinative attachment (al-ta'liq al-tanjizi al-qadim): God's eternal decision to specify a thing with its future attributes. The second is the pre-eternal potential attachment (al-ta'liq al-suluhi al-qadim): the will's inherent capacity to specify, while the actual specification is also eternal. Some scholars propose a third, the originated determinative attachment (al-ta'liq al-tanjizi al-hadith), which would be God's specification at the moment of creation. However, the author states that upon investigation, this third attachment is merely an external manifestation (izhar) of the first, not an independent attachment. This preserves the immutability of God's will: there is no new decision in time; the temporal act only executes the eternal decree.

Key Terms
تعلق الإرادة
تعلق تنجيزي قديم
تعلق صلوحي قديم
تعلق تنجيزي حادث
تحقيق

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that God's will changes or acquires new decisions at the moment of creation, implying a temporal attachment.
Correction: The author clarifies that any temporal act is only the manifestation of the eternal determinative attachment, not an independent attachment.

Checkpoint
What does the author say about the third attachment (al-ta'liq al-tanjizi al-hadith)?
A) It is independent and necessary
B) It is a manifestation of the pre-eternal determinative attachment
C) It is the only true attachment
D) It does not exist at all

Key Takeaway
The divine will has only two eternal attachments, and any temporal act is the manifestation of the eternal decree, not a new decision.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you truly believe that every event in your life—including your successes, failures, and trials—was pre-eternally determined by Allah's will, not as a reaction but as an eternal decree? How does this reality affect the way you respond to unexpected difficulties today? Commit to one specific situation where you will trust Allah’s eternal will over your immediate feelings.
?
The author refutes the idea that God's will changes. Is there any area of your life where you act as if God's will is reacting to your choices—perhaps by worrying about outcomes or feeling that your mistakes catch Allah by surprise? How can you align your thinking with the truth that Allah's eternal will already encompasses all that happens? Write a short prayer asking for help to internalize this.
?
Consider a recent event that disrupted your plans. How does understanding that Allah's eternal determinative attachment (al-ta'liq al-tanjizi al-qadim) specified that event even before it occurred change your perspective? What is one practical step you will take today to submit to that eternal decree rather than resist it?

The Will Specifies Among Six Types of Opposites
Sanusi enumerates six types of opposites and explains divine will's selective function.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the role of the divine will in specifying among six categories of opposites, illustrating that God's choice is selective among equally possible alternatives.

Core Idea
God's will chooses one of six opposite pairs for every created thing.

In this passage, Al-Sanusi explains how the divine will (al-iraadah) functions as a selective power. It does not create arbitrarily but chooses among specific pairs of opposites: existence vs. non-existence, attributes, times, places, directions, and magnitudes. Understanding this helps us appreciate God's wisdom and the contingency of creation.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once to get a sense of its flow. Then read the translation. Notice how Al-Sanusi defines the six categories with examples before explaining the will's role.
The passage moves from examples to general categories to application.
2
Identify the six types of opposites
List the six categories: existence vs. non-existence, attributes, times, places, directions, and magnitudes. See how each pair is illustrated with concrete examples like whiteness/blackness.
The order is: existence/non-existence, attributes, times, places, directions, magnitudes.
3
Understand how the will specifies
For each pair, the will selects one specific option to be actualized. For instance, it specifies 'existence' over 'non-existence' for a thing, and 'whiteness' over 'blackness' for a white object.
The will always chooses, but the choice is not random—it aligns with divine wisdom.
4
Reflect on the misconception
Some think God's will creates from nothing without needing to select among options. This passage shows that God's will involves deliberate choice among equally possible alternatives, highlighting His freedom and perfection.
This underscores that creation is not random but intentionally designed.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
يقابل بعضاً، فكونه أبيض مثلا يقابل كونَه أسودَ ، وبعض الأزمنة يقابل بعضا، فكونه في زمن الطوفان مثلا يقابل كونه في زمن سيّدنا محمد الله ، وبعض الأمكنة يقابل بعضاً، فكونه في مصر يقابل كونه في تونس مثلاً ، وبعض الجهات يقابل بعضاً، فكونه في جهة المشرق يقابل كونَه في جهة المغرب مثلاً ، وبعض المقادير يقابل بعضا، فكونه طويلا مثلا يقابل كونه قصيراً . الممكنات فقول النَّاظم في البيتين اللذين رويناهما لك: «وجودنا والعدم»، واحد من المتقابلات، وقوله: (والصفات»، واحدٌ ثانٍ ، والثَّالث: الأمكنة، والرابع: الأزمنة، والخامس الجهات، والسادس: المقادير . فالإرادة تخصص الوجود الذي هو أحد الطرفين بالوقوع دون العدم، أو تخصص العدمَ الذي هو الطرف الآخر بالوقوع دون الوجود، وتخصص الصفة المخصوصة كالبياض مثلا بالوقوع دون غيرها من الصفات، وتخصص الزمان المخصوص بالوقوع فيه دون غيره من الأزمنة ، وتخصص المكان المخصوص بالوقوع فيه دون غيره من الأمكنة، وتخصص الجهة المخصوصة بالوقوع فيها دون غيرها من الجهات، وتخصص المقدار المخصوص بالوقوع للجرم دون غيره من المقادير .
One opposes another: whiteness, for example, opposes blackness; and some times oppose others, like being in the time of the flood opposes being in the time of our master Muhammad ﷺ; and some places oppose others, like being in Egypt opposes being in Tunisia; and some directions oppose others, like being in the direction of the east opposes being in the direction of the west; and some magnitudes oppose others, like being long opposes being short. The possibles: the author's words in the two lines we have transmitted to you: 'Our existence and non-existence' is one of the opposites, and his words: 'and attributes' is a second, the third: places, the fourth: times, the fifth: directions, and the sixth: magnitudes. So the will specifies existence which is one of the two sides to occur rather than non-existence, or it specifies non-existence which is the other side to occur rather than existence; and it specifies the particular attribute like whiteness to occur rather than other attributes; and it specifies the particular time to occur in rather than other times; and it specifies the particular place to occur in rather than other places; and it specifies the particular direction to occur in rather than other directions; and it specifies the particular magnitude for the body to have rather than other magnitudes.
Source pages p. 241

Commentary
This passage defines the function of the divine will (al-iraadah) in specifying among alternatives. It first lists the six types of opposites (muttaqabilat) that are relevant: existence vs. non-existence, attributes, times, places, directions, and magnitudes. Each pair of opposites is illustrated by examples. Then it cites the text of the Jawharah indicating that existence/non-existence, attributes, etc., are the six categories. Finally, it explains that the will's role is to choose one of the two opposites for actualization: it specifies existence over non-existence, or vice versa (though normally existence is chosen); it specifies a particular attribute, time, place, direction, and magnitude for a thing. This shows that the will is selective among possibilities, not arbitrary.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think God's will creates from nothing without needing to choose among alternatives.
Correction: This passage shows that God's will involves deliberate selection among equally possible opposites, such as existence vs. non-existence, attributes, times, etc. Creation is not random but involves specific choices.

Checkpoint
According to this passage, how many types of opposites does the will specify among?
A) 3
B) 4
C) 5
D) 6

Key Takeaway
The divine will selects one alternative from each of six pairs of opposites for every created thing, showing that creation is a deliberate choice among possibilities.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
God's will chose for you to exist in this specific time and place. How does that truth change the way you view your daily struggles and blessings? What will you do today to live as if you were chosen on purpose?
?
If the divine will had specified a different attribute for you—less intelligence, weaker health, or a different disposition—would your worship be easier or harder? What does that tell you about your accountability for what you have been given?
?
You are alive now, not in the time of the flood or any other era. What responsibility does that specific timing place on you? How will you seize this moment to fulfill the purpose for which you were willed into existence?

The Two Attachments of the Divine Will
Al-Sanusi presents the two types of attachment of the will according to the verifying scholars.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the two attachments of the divine will as affirmed by the verifying scholars and why a third temporally originated attachment is not considered independent.

Core Idea
The divine will eternally attaches in two ways: determinate and suitability.

Sanusi now delves into a finer point of divine will: how it relates to its objects. The verifying scholars distinguish two eternal attachments—one that specifies each thing’s attributes, and another that is the will’s capacity to specify. Some proposed a third temporal attachment, but the verified view is that it is merely a manifestation of the eternal one. Additionally, the will only applies to possible beings, not to the necessary or impossible.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Begin by reading the Arabic passage and its English translation. Pay attention to the technical terms and the flow of the argument.
The passage is dense; focus on distinguishing the two attachments.
2
Identify the two main attachments
Find the definitions of the two attachments: the ancient determinate attachment (al-ta'alluq al-tanzili al-qadeem) and the ancient suitability attachment (al-ta'alluq al-saluhi al-qadeem). Notice how they differ.
The first is about what God knows the thing will be, the second is about the will's capacity to specify.
3
Understand the third attachment and its rejection
Some scholars propose a third attachment (originated determinate). Sanusi states the verification: it is not independent but a manifestation of the first.
This guards against thinking the will changes over time.
4
Note the scope of the will
The passage concludes that the divine will attaches only to possible beings. Reflect on why the necessary and impossible are excluded.
God’s will does not apply to what is already necessary (His essence) or what is impossible.

وأما بيان تعلق الإرادة فاعلم أولا: أنَّ الذي ذكره المحققون من تعلقات الإرادة تعلقين، الأَوَّل: التَّعلق التنجيزي القديم، وهو: تخصيص الله تعالى الشيء أزلا بالصفات التي يعلمُ أَنَّه يوجَدُ عليها في الخارج وقد سبق بيان هذا، والثاني: تعلق صلوحي قديم، وهو: صلاحية الإرادة في الأزل للتخصيص مع ثبوت التخصيص بالفعل أزلا أيضا» ومن العلماء من أثبت للإرادة تعلقاً ثالثًا، وسماه «التعلق التنجيزي الحادث» وهو: «تخصيص الله تعالى الشيء عند إيجاده بالفعل بما سبق تعلقها به «أزلا والتحقيق أنَّ هذا إظهار للتعلق التنجيزي القديم، وليس تعلقا مُستقلا واعلم ثانيا : أنَّ تعلق الإرادة بالممكن وحده لا بالواجب ولا المستحيل، وأنَّ الممكن=
As for the explanation of the attachment of the will, know first: that which the verifying scholars mentioned regarding the attachments of the will are two attachments: the first is the ancient determinate attachment (al-ta'alluq al-tanzili al-qadeem), which is: Allah's specification of the thing from eternity with the attributes upon which He knows it will exist in the external world, and this has been explained before. The second is the ancient suitability attachment (ta'alluq saluhi qadeem), which is: the fitness of the will from eternity for specification with the affirmation of specification de facto from eternity as well. And among scholars are those who affirmed a third attachment for the will, calling it 'the originated determinate attachment' (al-ta'alluq al-tanzili al-hadith), which is: 'Allah's specification of the thing at the time of its actual bringing into existence with what the will had previously attached to it from eternity.' And the verification is that this is a manifestation of the ancient determinate attachment, and not an independent attachment. And know secondly: that the will attaches only to the possible, not to the necessary nor the impossible, and that the possible= [incomplete]
Source pages p. 241

Commentary
This passage delves into the technical theory of how the divine will relates to its objects (ta'alluqat). It distinguishes two types of attachment affirmed by the verifying scholars (al-muhaqqiqoon). The first is the ancient determinate attachment (al-ta'alluq al-tanzili al-qadeem): God's eternal specification of each thing with the particular attributes it will have when it comes into existence. The second is the ancient suitability attachment (al-ta'alluq al-saluhi al-qadeem): the will's eternal capacity to specify, even though the actual specification is also eternal. Some scholars proposed a third attachment, called the originated determinate attachment (al-ta'alluq al-tanzili al-hadith), which refers to God's specification of the thing at the very moment of its creation, but the preferred view is that this is merely the manifestation of the first attachment, not a distinct one. Further, the passage states that the will only attaches to possible beings (mumkinat), not to the necessary (wajib) or the impossible (mustahil). The text ends with an incomplete sentence about the possible.

Key Terms

تنجيزي
صلوحي
قديم
حادث
ممكن
واجب
مستحيل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The divine will 'changes' or 'updates' when it creates things in time, implying a new attachment at the moment of creation.
Correction: The verifying scholars affirm that the will's attachments are eternal; the temporal act of creation is merely a manifestation of the eternal determinate attachment, not a new independent attachment.

Checkpoint
How many attachments of the will do the verifying scholars affirm?
A) One
B) Two
C) Three
D) Four

Key Takeaway
The divine will has two eternal attachments and applies only to possible beings.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
How does the truth that God's will is eternally fixed and unchanging challenge the way you react to unexpected events in your life? Can you point to a specific moment when you felt that things 'should have gone differently'—and how might this lesson reshape that feeling into trust in God's eternal specification?
?
The will attaches only to possible things, not to impossibilities. Does this limitation on what God can will (e.g., He cannot will to create a square circle) make God less powerful in your eyes, or does it reveal a deeper coherence in His nature? How does this understanding affect your awe of Him?
?
Some people think God's will changes as history unfolds. This lesson tells you that the originated attachment is just the appearance of the eternal will. Have you ever secretly believed that God changes His mind? What would it mean for your own humility if you truly internalized that His will never changes, and that every event was eternally determined by His wisdom? What one area of your life would you need to surrender to this truth starting today?

Distinction Between Will and Command
Al-Sanusi defines will and contrasts it with command through a debate.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that Allah's will is distinct from His internal command, and that God's will encompasses all events including evil, without commanding them directly.

Core Idea
Will and command are not the same; God may will what He does not command.

Al-Sanusi begins by clarifying that the divine will is different from the internal command (amr nafsi). He then narrates a famous debate between a Mu'tazili scholar and a Sunni scholar to illustrate the Sunni position that God's will covers all things, including evil, but that does not mean He commands evil.

Guided Path
1
Read the definition
Read the first sentence where Al-Sanusi states that will differs from the internal command. Note the definition of internal command as a requisition for action, general for good or evil.
The internal command is a conceptual command, not a spoken one.
2
Follow the debate
Read the debate between Qadi Abd al-Jabbar and Abu Ishaq. Notice how each scholar's statement reflects their theological position.
The debate centers on whether God wills evil and whether that implies injustice.
3
Identify the Sunni stance
From Abu Ishaq's replies, deduce that Sunnis affirm that God wills all that occurs, but distinguish will from command and from pleasure.
4
Reflect on the lesson
Consider why this distinction matters for understanding divine justice and responsibility.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
2 - صفة الإرادة (وغَايَرَتْ) الإرادةُ :أي: خالفتْ (أَمْرًا) نفسيَّا(۱)، وهو: اقتضاءُ فعل = غير أعمُ مِن أنْ يكونَ خيرًا أو شرًا، وخالف في هذا الأخير المعتزلة، فزعموا أن إرادة الله لا تتعلق بالشرور ولا القبائح ، ويُروى أنَّ القاضي عبد الجبار الهمداني دخل على الصاحب بن عباد ، فوجد عنده الأستاذ أبا إسحاق الإسفراييني ، والقاضي عبد الجبار من رؤوس المعتزلة ، والأستاذ أبو إسحاق من أكابر أهل السُّنة ، فقال القاضي عبد الجبار: سبحان من تنزه عن الفحشاء، يريد التنديد بأهل السنة الذين قالوا: إن الشَّرَ واقع من الناس بإرادة الله تعالى، فقال الأستاذ أبو إسحاق: سبحان من لا يجري في ملكه إلا ما يشاء، فقال القاضي عبد الجبار : أَفَيُريدُ ربُّنا أن يُعْصَى ؟! فقال الأستاذ أبو إسحاق أَفَيُعْصَى رَبُّنَا كُرها ؟! فقال القاضي عبد الجبار : أرأيتَ إن منعني الهُدى وقضى علي بالرِّدَى ، أَحسن إلي أم أساءَ ؟ فقال الأستاذ أبو إسحاق: إن منعكَ ما هو لك فقد أساء، وإن منعك ما هو له فهو يخص برحمته من يشاء، وانقطع الحديث بعد ذلك

والذي ذهب إليه المعتزلة من تخصيص الممكن الذي تتعلق الإرادة به بالخير ؛ مبني على ما ذهبوا إليه من أنَّ الإرادة توافقُ الرِّضَا في المعنى، والحقُّ ما ذهب إليه أهل السنة الرضا غير الإرادة وسيأتي إيضاح ذلك في الجهة الرابعة من الكلام على هذه الصفة أنَّ أهل السنة يرون أنَّ إرادة الله تتعلق بكل ممكن خيرًا كان ذلك الممكن أو شرا؛ فإنهم يختلفون في جواز نسبة الشرور والقبائح إليه تعالى ، والذي رجّحه المحققون: جواز ذلك في مقام التّعليم دون غيره، وهذا الخلاف جارٍ أيضًا في نسبة الأمور الخسيسة إليه تعالى والأصح أيضًا جواز ذلك في مقام التعليم دون غيره، فلا يجوز أن تقول: الله خالق القردة والخنازير ، إلا أن يكون ذلك في مقام التعليم، وهذا ضرب من الأدب ليس غير محي.

(۱) هذا الموضوع هو الجهة الرابعة من جهات الكلام على صفة الإرادة ، وحاصله أنَّ مذهب أهل السنة والجماعة أنَّ إرادة الله تعالى ليست عين أمره، ولا هي مستلزمة له، فقد يريد الشيء ويأمرُ به، كأمره بالإيمان من علم الله تعالى منهم الإيمان، فإنه أراده منهم وأمرهم به ، وقد لا يريد الشيء ولا يأمرُ به ككفر من علم الله إيمانهم، فإنه سبحانه لم يرده ولم يأمرهم به ، وقد يريد الله تعالى الشيء ولا يأمر به ككفر أبي لهب مثلا، وكالمعاصي من آحاد الناس، فإن الله جلَّتْ قدرته قد أراد ذلك كله ، ولكنه سبحانه لم يأمر به، بل أمر بأضداده ، فقد أمر أبا لهب بالإيمان، وأمر سائر الناس بالطاعات ، وقد يأمر الله تعالى ء ولا يريده كالإيمان من أبي لهب والطاعة من عامة العصاة ، فإنه سبحانه أمر أبا لهب=
بما أمر به
2 - The Attribute of Will: And the will differs from an internal command. The internal command is the requisition of an action, general, whether it be good or evil. The Mu'tazila opposed in the latter, claiming that Allah's will does not attach to evil acts or abominations. It is narrated that al-Qadi Abd al-Jabbar al-Hamadani entered upon al-Sahib ibn Abbad and found with him al-Ustadh Abu Ishaq al-Isfarayini. Al-Qadi Abd al-Jabbar was one of the heads of the Mu'tazila, and al-Ustadh Abu Ishaq was among the greats of the Ahl al-Sunnah. Al-Qadi Abd al-Jabbar said: 'Glorified is He who is transcendent above obscenity,' intending to criticize the Ahl al-Sunnah who said that evil occurs from people by Allah's will. Al-Ustadh Abu Ishaq said: 'Glorified is He whose dominion nothing occurs except what He wills.' Al-Qadi Abd al-Jabbar said: 'Does our Lord then will to be disobeyed?' Al-Ustadh Abu Ishaq said: 'Is our Lord then disobeyed by force?' Al-Qadi Abd al-Jabbar said: 'Tell me, if He prevents me from guidance and decrees upon me ruin, has He done good to me or wronged me?' Al-Ustadh Abu Ishaq said: 'If He prevented what is yours, then He wronged; but if He prevented what is His, then He bestows His mercy upon whom He wills.' The discussion ended after that. And what the Mu'tazila held regarding the specification of the possible to which will attaches to only good is based on their view that will coincides with pleasure (rida) in meaning. The truth is what the Ahl al-Sunnah hold: pleasure is other than will. Clarification will come in the fourth aspect of discussion on this attribute. Indeed, the Ahl al-Sunnah hold that Allah's will attaches to every possible, whether that possible is good or evil. However, they differ on the permissibility of attributing evils and abominations to Him. What the verifying scholars preferred is that it is permissible in the context of teaching (maqam al-ta'lim) but not otherwise. This difference also applies to attributing lowly things to Him. The more correct view is also that it is permissible in the teaching context only. So one should not say 'Allah is the Creator of monkeys and pigs' except in a teaching context. This is a form of etiquette, nothing more. 
Footnote (1): 'This topic is the fourth aspect of the aspects of discourse on the attribute of will. Its summary is that the school of Ahl al-Sunnah wa al-Jama'ah is that Allah's will is not the same as His command, nor does it entail it. So sometimes He wills a thing and commands it, like His command to faith for those from whom Allah knew faith, for He willed it from them and commanded them with it. And sometimes He does not will a thing nor command it, like the disbelief of those from whom Allah knew faith, for He did not will it nor command them with it. And sometimes Allah wills a thing but does not command it, like the disbelief of Abu Lahab for example, and like the sins of individual people. Indeed, Allah, exalted is His power, has willed all of that, but He did not command it; rather He commanded its opposites. So He commanded Abu Lahab to believe, and He commanded all people to obey. And sometimes Allah commands a thing but does not will it, like faith from Abu Lahab and obedience from the general sinners. For He commanded Abu Lahab with what He commanded.' [end]
Source pages p. 242

Commentary
This passage covers several key points about the divine will. First, it distinguishes will from the internal command (amr nafsi), noting that the command is a requital for an action whether good or evil, while will is different. The Mu'tazilites opposed, saying God does not will evil. A famous debate illustrates the Sunni position: when asked if God wills to be disobeyed, Abu Ishaq replies that God is not disobeyed by force, implying that His will is effective. The debate shows the Sunni commitment to God's all-encompassing will. Then the passage explains that the Mu'tazilite view that will attaches only to good is based on equating will with pleasure (rida). The Sunni view is that they are distinct; God may will something but not be pleased with it. Then there is a discussion about attributing evil to God: it is permissible only in a teaching context, as a form of etiquette (adab). The footnote expands on the fourth aspect: the distinction between will and command, giving four logical possibilities: (1) will and command together (e.g., faith of believers), (2) neither (e.g., disbelief of those from whom God knew faith), (3) will without command (e.g., Abu Lahab's disbelief, sins), (4) command without will (e.g., faith from Abu Lahab, obedience from sinners). This shows that will and command are not coextensive.

Key Terms
إرادة
أمر نفسي
المعتزلة
أهل السنة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that God's will and His command are identical, so if God wills something He must also command it.
Correction: The passage clarifies that will and command are distinct: God may will something (like Abu Lahab's disbelief) without commanding it, and may command something (like faith from Abu Lahab) without willing it.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the relation between God's will and evil acts?
A) God does not will evil acts at all.
B) God wills evil acts but does not command them.
C) God wills and commands evil acts.
D) God neither wills nor commands evil acts.

Key Takeaway
God's will is all-encompassing and distinct from His command; He may will what He does not command.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you sin or experience hardship, do you truly believe it occurs by God's will, or do you feel that it somehow escapes His control? How does this belief affect your repentance and trust in Him?
?
If you knew that every act of disobedience you commit is willed by God, would you still feel personally accountable? Why or why not?
?
The Mu'tazili question about God preventing guidance touches on feelings of injustice. Have you ever felt that God owes you something—like guidance or a good life? How does Abu Ishaq's response challenge that assumption?

Does God Will Evil? The Mu'tazili Error and the Sunni Doctrine of Etiquette
Al-Sanusi discusses the Mu'tazili error and the etiquette of attributing evil to God.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the Mu'tazila erroneously equated will with divine pleasure, and why Sunnis consider it permissible to attribute evil to God only in teaching contexts.

Core Idea
God may will what He dislikes; evil is attributed to Him only for teaching, not in ordinary speech.

Al-Sanusi explains that the Mu'tazila mistakenly identified God's will with His pleasure (rida). He then clarifies the Sunni position: God's will attaches to all possibilities, good or evil, but it is a matter of etiquette (adab) to avoid directly attributing evil to God except in educational settings.

Guided Path
1
Read the explanation of the Mu'tazili error
Read the passage that begins 'والذي ذهب إليه المعتزلة' and note why they restricted God's will to good.
Their mistake was equating will with pleasure.
2
Understand the Sunni correction
Read that the truth is that pleasure is other than will. This allows God to will things He is not pleased with.
3
Learn the ruling on attributing evil to God
Read about the disagreement among Sunnis and the preferred view: it is permissible only in a teaching context, as a form of etiquette.
Example: saying 'Allah is the Creator of pigs' is allowed when teaching.

And sometimes He does not will a thing nor command it, like the disbelief of those from whom Allah knew faith, for He did not will it nor command them with it. And sometimes Allah wills a thing but does not command it, like the disbelief of Abu Lahab for example, and like the sins of individual people. Indeed, Allah, exalted is His power, has willed all of that, but He did not command it; rather He commanded its opposites. So He commanded Abu Lahab to believe, and He commanded all people to obey. And sometimes Allah commands a thing but does not will it, like faith from Abu Lahab and obedience from the general sinners. For He commanded Abu Lahab with what He commanded.' [end]
Source pages p. 242

Commentary
This passage covers several key points about the divine will. First, it distinguishes will from the internal command (amr nafsi), noting that the command is a requital for an action whether good or evil, while will is different. The Mu'tazilites opposed, saying God does not will evil. A famous debate illustrates the Sunni position: when asked if God wills to be disobeyed, Abu Ishaq replies that God is not disobeyed by force, implying that His will is effective. The debate shows the Sunni commitment to God's all-encompassing will. Then the passage explains that the Mu'tazilite view that will attaches only to good is based on equating will with pleasure (rida). The Sunni view is that they are distinct; God may will something but not be pleased with it. Then there is a discussion about attributing evil to God: it is permissible only in a teaching context, as a form of etiquette (adab). The footnote expands on the fourth aspect: the distinction between will and command, giving four logical possibilities: (1) will and command together (e.g., faith of believers), (2) neither (e.g., disbelief of those from whom God knew faith), (3) will without command (e.g., Abu Lahab's disbelief, sins), (4) command without will (e.g., faith from Abu Lahab, obedience from sinners). This shows that will and command are not coextensive.

Key Terms

رضا
الشرور
القبائح
مقام التعليم
المعتزلة
أهل السنة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That God's will and pleasure are identical, so God cannot will evil.
Correction: Sunnis hold that will and pleasure are distinct; God may will evil without being pleased with it.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, why did the Mu'tazila mistakenly restrict God's will to good?
A) Because they denied God's power over evil.
B) Because they equated will with pleasure.
C) Because they believed evil does not exist.
D) Because they followed a different Quranic interpretation.

Key Takeaway
God's will encompasses all, but attributing evil to Him is a matter of adab, permitted only for teaching.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you witness suffering or evil in the world, do you ever find yourself thoughtlessly attributing it to Allah? How can you train your tongue to speak with the adab this lesson demands?
?
This lesson shows that Allah may will what He does not please. Is there any sin or shortcoming in your life that you have excused by thinking 'Allah willed it, so it must be okay'? How will you align your heart with the distinction between will and pleasure?
?
The verifying scholars permitted attributing evil to Allah only in teaching contexts. Are there areas of your daily speech—jokes, complaints, or casual remarks—where you speak too loosely about Allah? What specific change will you make today to honor His majesty?

Four Logical Combinations of Will and Command
Al-Sanusi enumerates four logical possibilities between will and command.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the four logical possibilities between God's will and His command, and how they clarify divine decree and human responsibility.

Core Idea
Will and command are independent; four combinations cover all relations between them.

In this footnote, Al-Sanusi expands on the fourth aspect of the attribute of will. He presents a logical enumeration of the four possible relations between God's will and His command, using concrete examples from faith and disbelief to illustrate each case.

Guided Path
1
Read the four combinations
Read the footnote carefully. Identify each of the four cases: (1) will and command together, (2) neither, (3) will without command, (4) command without will.
The examples given are specific to believers and disbelievers.
2
Match each combination to its example
For each case, note the example: (1) faith of believers, (2) disbelief of those who later believe, (3) disbelief of Abu Lahab and sins, (4) faith of Abu Lahab and obedience of sinners.
3
Understand the implication
Reflect on how this shows that God's will is not always accompanied by command, and command is not always accompanied by will.
This reinforces the earlier distinction between will and command.

بما أمر به
2 - The Attribute of Will: And the will differs from an internal command. The internal command is the requisition of an action, general, whether it be good or evil. The Mu'tazila opposed in the latter, claiming that Allah's will does not attach to evil acts or abominations. It is narrated that al-Qadi Abd al-Jabbar al-Hamadani entered upon al-Sahib ibn Abbad and found with him al-Ustadh Abu Ishaq al-Isfarayini. Al-Qadi Abd al-Jabbar was one of the heads of the Mu'tazila, and al-Ustadh Abu Ishaq was among the greats of the Ahl al-Sunnah. Al-Qadi Abd al-Jabbar said: 'Glorified is He who is transcendent above obscenity,' intending to criticize the Ahl al-Sunnah who said that evil occurs from people by Allah's will. Al-Ustadh Abu Ishaq said: 'Glorified is He whose dominion nothing occurs except what He wills.' Al-Qadi Abd al-Jabbar said: 'Does our Lord then will to be disobeyed?' Al-Ustadh Abu Ishaq said: 'Is our Lord then disobeyed by force?' Al-Qadi Abd al-Jabbar said: 'Tell me, if He prevents me from guidance and decrees upon me ruin, has He done good to me or wronged me?' Al-Ustadh Abu Ishaq said: 'If He prevented what is yours, then He wronged; but if He prevented what is His, then He bestows His mercy upon whom He wills.' The discussion ended after that. And what the Mu'tazila held regarding the specification of the possible to which will attaches to only good is based on their view that will coincides with pleasure (rida) in meaning. The truth is what the Ahl al-Sunnah hold: pleasure is other than will. Clarification will come in the fourth aspect of discussion on this attribute. Indeed, the Ahl al-Sunnah hold that Allah's will attaches to every possible, whether that possible is good or evil. However, they differ on the permissibility of attributing evils and abominations to Him. What the verifying scholars preferred is that it is permissible in the context of teaching (maqam al-ta'lim) but not otherwise. This difference also applies to attributing lowly things to Him. The more correct view is also that it is permissible in the teaching context only. So one should not say 'Allah is the Creator of monkeys and pigs' except in a teaching context. This is a form of etiquette, nothing more. 
Footnote (1): 'This topic is the fourth aspect of the aspects of discourse on the attribute of will. Its summary is that the school of Ahl al-Sunnah wa al-Jama'ah is that Allah's will is not the same as His command, nor does it entail it. So sometimes He wills a thing and commands it, like His command to faith for those from whom Allah knew faith, for He willed it from them and commanded them with it. And sometimes He does not will a thing nor command it, like the disbelief of those from whom Allah knew faith, for He did not will it nor command them with it. And sometimes Allah wills a thing but does not command it, like the disbelief of Abu Lahab for example, and like the sins of individual people. Indeed, Allah, exalted is His power, has willed all of that, but He did not command it; rather He commanded its opposites. So He commanded Abu Lahab to believe, and He commanded all people to obey. And sometimes Allah commands a thing but does not will it, like faith from Abu Lahab and obedience from the general sinners. For He commanded Abu Lahab with what He commanded.' [end]
Source pages p. 242

Commentary
This passage covers several key points about the divine will. First, it distinguishes will from the internal command (amr nafsi), noting that the command is a requital for an action whether good or evil, while will is different. The Mu'tazilites opposed, saying God does not will evil. A famous debate illustrates the Sunni position: when asked if God wills to be disobeyed, Abu Ishaq replies that God is not disobeyed by force, implying that His will is effective. The debate shows the Sunni commitment to God's all-encompassing will. Then the passage explains that the Mu'tazilite view that will attaches only to good is based on equating will with pleasure (rida). The Sunni view is that they are distinct; God may will something but not be pleased with it. Then there is a discussion about attributing evil to God: it is permissible only in a teaching context, as a form of etiquette (adab). The footnote expands on the fourth aspect: the distinction between will and command, giving four logical possibilities: (1) will and command together (e.g., faith of believers), (2) neither (e.g., disbelief of those from whom God knew faith), (3) will without command (e.g., Abu Lahab's disbelief, sins), (4) command without will (e.g., faith from Abu Lahab, obedience from sinners). This shows that will and command are not coextensive.

The Attribute of Will and Its Four Cases
Definition and enumeration of four cases of will-command relationship

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that divine will is distinct from divine command and the four possible relationships between them.

Core Idea
God's will and command are not the same; they can align or diverge in four ways.

In this passage, Sanusi establishes a crucial distinction between God's will (al-irada) and His command (al-amr). He begins by defining al-irada and then outlines four cases that demonstrate how the two relate. This sets the foundation for understanding why God may command something He does not will to happen.

Guided Path
1
Read the definition and identify the distinction
Read the first part of the passage where al-irada is defined as differing from 'amr nafsi. Understand that al-irada is broader, covering good and evil.
The term 'amr nafsi means a demand for an action by another.
2
Enumerate the four cases
Identify the four cases listed: God wills and commands; God does not will and does not command; God wills but does not command; God commands but does not will.
Each case is illustrated with an example from the passage.
3
Connect cases to examples
For each case, note the example given: believers' faith, disbelief of the faithful, disbelief of Abu Lahab, faith of Abu Lahab. Ensure you can match each example to its case.
4
Reflect on the theological significance
Consider why this distinction matters: it preserves God's sovereignty and wisdom, allowing Him to command for purposes beyond mere compliance.
This leads into the objection and answer in the next lesson.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
٢ - صفة الإرادة (وَغَايَرَتْ) الإرادةُ :أي: خالفتْ (أَمْرًا) نفسيَّا، وهو: اقتضاءُ فعل غير أعمُ مِن أنْ يكونَ خيرًا أو شرًا. هذا الموضوع هو الجهة الرابعة من جهات الكلام على صفة الإرادة ، وحاصله أنَّ مذهب أهل السنة والجماعة أنَّ إرادة الله تعالى ليست عين أمره، ولا هي مستلزمة له، فقد يريد الشيء ويأمرُ به، كأمره بالإيمان من علم الله تعالى منهم الإيمان، فإنه أراده منهم وأمرهم به ، وقد لا يريد الشيء ولا يأمرُ به ككفر من علم الله إيمانهم، فإنه سبحانه لم يرده ولم يأمرهم به ، وقد يريد الله تعالى الشيء ولا يأمر به ككفر أبي لهب مثلا، وكالمعاصي من آحاد الناس، فإن الله جلَّتْ قدرته قد أراد ذلك كله ، ولكنه سبحانه لم يأمر به، بل أمر بأضداده ، فقد أمر أبا لهب بالإيمان، وأمر سائر الناس بالطاعات ، وقد يأمر الله تعالى ولا يريده كالإيمان من أبي لهب والطاعة من عامة العصاة ، فإنه سبحانه أمر أبا لهب بالإيمان ولم يرده منه ، وأمر عامة المذنبين بالطاعة ولم يردها منهم . فإن قلت فكيف يأمر الله تعالى بشيء وهو مريد لعدم حصوله ؟ فالجواب: أن فائدة الأمر لم تنحصر في رغبة الامتثال ، بل يجوز أن تكون ثَمَّة حكمةٌ غابت وهي معلومة له سبحانه ، على أنا ندرك بعض حكم الله في أوامره التي منها قطع الحجة على الكفَّار والعصاة حتّى لا يقول أحدهم : لو كلفتني لَقُمْتُ بما كلفتنيه.
2 - The Attribute of Will (al-Irada). And (ghayarat) al-Irada means: it differed from (amran) nafsiyyan, which is: the demand for an action of another, more general than being good or evil. This topic is the fourth aspect (al-jiha al-rabi'a) of the aspects of discourse on the attribute of will, and its summary is that the doctrine of Ahl al-Sunna wa al-Jama'a is that the will of Allah Most High is not identical to His command, nor does it necessitate it. For He may will a thing and command it, like His command of faith from those of whom Allah knew faith, for He willed it from them and commanded them with it. And He may not will a thing and not command it, like the disbelief of those of whom Allah knew their faith, for He did not will it nor command them with it. And Allah may will a thing and not command it, like the disbelief of Abu Lahab for example, and like the acts of disobedience of individual people; for Allah, mighty is His power, has willed all of that, but He did not command it; rather, He commanded their opposites. For He commanded Abu Lahab with faith and commanded all people with acts of obedience. And Allah may command and not will it, like the faith of Abu Lahab and the obedience of the general run of sinners; for He commanded Abu Lahab with faith and did not will it from him, and commanded the general run of sinners with obedience and did not will it from them. If you say: How can Allah command a thing while He wills that it not happen? The answer: The benefit of the command is not limited to the desire for compliance; rather, it is possible that there is a wisdom that is hidden, known to Him. Indeed, we perceive some of Allah's wisdom in His commands, including cutting off the argument against the disbelievers and sinners so that none of them may say: 'If You had obligated me, I would have done what You obligated me.'
Source pages p. 242

Commentary
This passage is a definition and explanation of the attribute of divine will (al-irada) and its relationship to divine command (al-amr). The author begins by defining al-irada as differing from a 'nafsi command' (amr nafsi), which is the demand for an action by another, encompassing both good and evil. This definition already signals a key point: divine will is broader than command. The author then identifies this as the 'fourth aspect' of discourse on the attribute, and lays out the Sunni position: God's will is neither identical to His command nor necessarily concomitant with it. Four cases are enumerated: (1) God wills and commands, e.g., the faith of believers; (2) God does not will and does not command, e.g., the disbelief of those who will believe? Actually the example is 'kufr of those whose faith Allah knows' meaning those destined for faith—their disbelief is not willed nor commanded; (3) God wills but does not command, e.g., the disbelief of Abu Lahab and sins of individuals; (4) God commands but does not will, e.g., faith from Abu Lahab and obedience from sinners. The author then anticipates an objection: How can God command what He does not will to happen? The answer is that the purpose of command is not solely to obtain compliance; there may be hidden wisdoms known to God, and we can perceive some, such as cutting off excuse so that no one can claim they would have obeyed if commanded. This section emphasizes the distinction between irada and amr and justifies God's commands that are not fulfilled.

Key Terms
الإرادة
الأمر النفسي

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think God's will and command always coincide or that one necessarily implies the other.
Correction: The passage shows they are distinct and can be in any combination of two binary oppositions (will/no will and command/no command).

Checkpoint
According to the passage, which of the following is NOT one of the four cases regarding God's will and command?
A) God wills and commands
B) God wills but does not command
C) God wills and forbids
D) God commands but does not will

Key Takeaway
God's will and command are distinct; they can align or diverge in four possible ways, each illustrated by clear examples.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If God wills your sins but does not command them, are you more or less responsible for your disobedience? How does this truth change the way you view your own choices and repentance?
?
Consider a sin you struggle with. Knowing that God commands you to avoid it but may have willed its occurrence for a wise purpose, how does that affect your reliance on His mercy and your resolve to obey?
?
Abu Lahab was commanded to believe even though God did not will faith from him. Does this disturb you? How does it shape your gratitude for the faith God has willed for you?

Answering the Objection: Why God Commands What He Does Not Will
Objection and answer regarding the purpose of divine command

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Sunni response to the objection that God cannot command what He does not will.

Core Idea
Commands have purposes beyond mere compliance, including cutting off excuses.

An objection arises from the four cases: if God commands something but does not will it, how is that consistent? Sanusi provides the answer by explaining that commands serve multiple purposes, including establishing a proof against humanity. This lesson examines that response.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection aloud
The objection asks: How can God command something while willing that it not happen? Understand the logical tension.
This objection naturally follows from the fourth case presented earlier.
2
Identify the core of the answer
Sanusi responds that the benefit of command is not limited to obtaining compliance. There can be hidden wisdoms.
He gives a specific example: cutting off the excuse of disbelievers.
3
Reflect on the example of cutting off excuses
If God had not commanded faith, disbelievers could claim they would have obeyed if commanded. By commanding, God removes that excuse.
This shows divine justice even when command is not fulfilled.
4
Consider the broader principle
Commands can serve multiple purposes, and God's wisdom is not limited to what we perceive.
This reinforces the distinction between will and command from the previous lesson.

٢ - صفة الإرادة (وَغَايَرَتْ) الإرادةُ :أي: خالفتْ (أَمْرًا) نفسيَّا، وهو: اقتضاءُ فعل غير أعمُ مِن أنْ يكونَ خيرًا أو شرًا. هذا الموضوع هو الجهة الرابعة من جهات الكلام على صفة الإرادة ، وحاصله أنَّ مذهب أهل السنة والجماعة أنَّ إرادة الله تعالى ليست عين أمره، ولا هي مستلزمة له، فقد يريد الشيء ويأمرُ به، كأمره بالإيمان من علم الله تعالى منهم الإيمان، فإنه أراده منهم وأمرهم به ، وقد لا يريد الشيء ولا يأمرُ به ككفر من علم الله إيمانهم، فإنه سبحانه لم يرده ولم يأمرهم به ، وقد يريد الله تعالى الشيء ولا يأمر به ككفر أبي لهب مثلا، وكالمعاصي من آحاد الناس، فإن الله جلَّتْ قدرته قد أراد ذلك كله ، ولكنه سبحانه لم يأمر به، بل أمر بأضداده ، فقد أمر أبا لهب بالإيمان، وأمر سائر الناس بالطاعات ، وقد يأمر الله تعالى ولا يريده كالإيمان من أبي لهب والطاعة من عامة العصاة ، فإنه سبحانه أمر أبا لهب بالإيمان ولم يرده منه ، وأمر عامة المذنبين بالطاعة ولم يردها منهم . فإن قلت فكيف يأمر الله تعالى بشيء وهو مريد لعدم حصوله ؟ فالجواب: أن فائدة الأمر لم تنحصر في رغبة الامتثال ، بل يجوز أن تكون ثَمَّة حكمةٌ غابت وهي معلومة له سبحانه ، على أنا ندرك بعض حكم الله في أوامره التي منها قطع الحجة على الكفَّار والعصاة حتّى لا يقول أحدهم : لو كلفتني لَقُمْتُ بما كلفتنيه.
2 - The Attribute of Will (al-Irada). And (ghayarat) al-Irada means: it differed from (amran) nafsiyyan, which is: the demand for an action of another, more general than being good or evil. This topic is the fourth aspect (al-jiha al-rabi'a) of the aspects of discourse on the attribute of will, and its summary is that the doctrine of Ahl al-Sunna wa al-Jama'a is that the will of Allah Most High is not identical to His command, nor does it necessitate it. For He may will a thing and command it, like His command of faith from those of whom Allah knew faith, for He willed it from them and commanded them with it. And He may not will a thing and not command it, like the disbelief of those of whom Allah knew their faith, for He did not will it nor command them with it. And Allah may will a thing and not command it, like the disbelief of Abu Lahab for example, and like the acts of disobedience of individual people; for Allah, mighty is His power, has willed all of that, but He did not command it; rather, He commanded their opposites. For He commanded Abu Lahab with faith and commanded all people with acts of obedience. And Allah may command and not will it, like the faith of Abu Lahab and the obedience of the general run of sinners; for He commanded Abu Lahab with faith and did not will it from him, and commanded the general run of sinners with obedience and did not will it from them. If you say: How can Allah command a thing while He wills that it not happen? The answer: The benefit of the command is not limited to the desire for compliance; rather, it is possible that there is a wisdom that is hidden, known to Him. Indeed, we perceive some of Allah's wisdom in His commands, including cutting off the argument against the disbelievers and sinners so that none of them may say: 'If You had obligated me, I would have done what You obligated me.'
Source pages p. 242

Commentary
This passage is a definition and explanation of the attribute of divine will (al-irada) and its relationship to divine command (al-amr). The author begins by defining al-irada as differing from a 'nafsi command' (amr nafsi), which is the demand for an action by another, encompassing both good and evil. This definition already signals a key point: divine will is broader than command. The author then identifies this as the 'fourth aspect' of discourse on the attribute, and lays out the Sunni position: God's will is neither identical to His command nor necessarily concomitant with it. Four cases are enumerated: (1) God wills and commands, e.g., the faith of believers; (2) God does not will and does not command, e.g., the disbelief of those who will believe? Actually the example is 'kufr of those whose faith Allah knows' meaning those destined for faith—their disbelief is not willed nor commanded; (3) God wills but does not command, e.g., the disbelief of Abu Lahab and sins of individuals; (4) God commands but does not will, e.g., faith from Abu Lahab and obedience from sinners. The author then anticipates an objection: How can God command what He does not will to happen? The answer is that the purpose of command is not solely to obtain compliance; there may be hidden wisdoms known to God, and we can perceive some, such as cutting off excuse so that no one can claim they would have obeyed if commanded. This section emphasizes the distinction between irada and amr and justifies God's commands that are not fulfilled.

Key Terms
الإرادة
الأمر النفسي
أهل السنة والجماعة
الجهة الرابعة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is one wisdom behind God's command of things He does not will?
A) To test faith severely
B) To cut off the argument of disbelievers
C) To increase punishment
D) To display divine power

Key Takeaway
God's commands have multiple wisdoms; one is to remove any excuse from the disbelievers, demonstrating divine justice.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever made an excuse for not obeying a divine command by telling yourself that God didn't give you the strength or opportunity? How does this passage directly strip away that excuse from your heart?
?
If you truly believed that every command of Allah has a hidden wisdom even when you fail to comply, how would that change your attitude toward the commands you find difficult or impossible to keep? What would you do differently starting today?
?
This lesson reveals that God commands things He does not will in order to cut off your excuses. Are there excuses you still cling to regarding your obedience? Will you let this truth remove them, or will you hold onto them even though you know they are invalid?

The Debate on Divine Will
A debate between a Mu'tazilite and a Sunni about divine will and evil.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the Sunni position on divine will is defended through a famous debate and why God's will encompasses all events, including evil.

Core Idea
God's will is absolute; nothing occurs in His dominion except what He wills.

This lesson presents a historical debate between a Mu'tazilite leader and a Sunni scholar that crystallizes the core difference on divine will. The exchange reveals the logical force of the Sunni position that God's will is not limited to good actions alone.

Guided Path
1
Read the debate dialogue
Read the Arabic passage and translation carefully. Follow the back-and-forth between al-Qadi 'Abd al-Jabbar and Abu Ishaq al-Isfara'ini.
Notice how each response builds on the previous.
2
Identify the key statements
Identify the Mu'tazilite's opening statement about transcendence, and Abu Ishaq's response about sovereignty. Then examine the cross-examination on disobedience and guidance.
3
Reflect on the ownership argument
Abu Ishaq's final response distinguishes between what is 'yours' and what is 'His'. Consider why this distinction is crucial for understanding divine justice.
4
Understand the significance
This debate illustrates the fundamental Sunni principle that God's will is unrestricted and that attributing evil to His will does not imply injustice.
