The Necessity and Proof of Allah's Attribute of Will
Sanusi defines the attribute of will and proves it from free agency.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the rational proof for Allah's attribute of will and how Sanusi resolves the objection about the simile in the poem.

Core Idea
Allah's will is proven by His free agency and distinct from other attributes.

This lesson examines Sanusi's argument for the divine attribute of will. He begins by stating its necessity within Ahl al-Sunna, then provides a rational proof based on Allah's free choice. He also anticipates and resolves a subtle objection concerning the grammatical comparison in the poem. The passage illustrates how kalam reasoning integrates textual evidence and logical analysis.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the full Arabic and translation to grasp the flow from necessity, proof, objection, and answer.
The passage includes a grammatical discussion.
2
Identify the proof structure
Notice that Sanusi first establishes the necessity, then provides the proof from free agency, and defines will. Then he addresses an objection.
3
Understand the objection and answer
The objection claims the simile collapses into identity. Sanusi's answer involves implicit relations (tacit relations) and distinguishes rational vs legal distinction.
4
Consider the alternative interpretation
Sanusi offers another possibility for the particle 'ka' to mean causation, shifting the meaning.
5
Reflect on the overall argument
See how Sanusi combines consensus, scriptural evidence, and rational analysis.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قسم الإلهيات في كونه بالضّرورة عند أهل السُّنَّة ; لأنَّه اتفق على إطلاق القول بأنَّه تعالى مُريد، وشاع ذلك في كلامه تعالى، وكلام أنبيائه عليهم الصَّلاة والسَّلام، ودلّ عليه ما ثبتَ مِن كونه تعالى فاعلا بالاختيار ; لأنَّ معناه: القصد والإرادة ملاحظة ما للطَّرَف الآخر، فكأنَّ المُختار ينظر إلى مع الطَّرفين ويميل إلى أحدهما، والمريدُ ينظر إلى الطرف الذي يريده، فإن قلت: فعلى هذا التقدير يتحد المشبَّه والمشبه به ، لأن المشبَّه هو التَّغاير وهو أيضا المشبه به ، وهذا لا معنى له . فالجواب عن هذا أن للمشبّه متعلقا محذوفا، وللمشبه به متعلقا محذوفا أيضا، والمتعلقان مختلفان ، وبهما يختلف المشبه والمشبه به، وتقدير الكلام وغايرت الإرادة الأمر والعلم والرضًا شرعاً تغايرا كائناً كالتغير الذي ثبت عقلا، فالمشبه به هو: التغاير العقلي، والمشبَّه : التَّغاير الشَّرعي، ويجوز في الكاف وجه آخر ، وهو أن يكون حرفا دالا على التعليل، ويكون (ما) اسما موصولاً مرادا به الدليل، وكأنه قال : وغايرت الإرادة الأمر والعلم والرضا هو: للدليل الذي ثبت.
Section on Divine Matters. Regarding its being necessarily [known] according to Ahl al-Sunna, because there is consensus on the absolute statement that He, exalted, is willing, and that is widespread in His speech, exalted, and in the speech of His prophets, peace and blessings be upon them, and it is indicated by what has been established of His being, exalted, an agent by choice; because its meaning is: the intention and will [involve] taking into consideration the other side. Thus, the one who chooses looks at both sides and inclines to one of them, and the one who wills looks to the side that he wills. If you say: on this estimation, the thing compared and that with which it is compared become the same, because the thing compared is the distinction and it is also that with which it is compared, and this has no meaning. The answer to this is: the thing compared has a deleted relation, and that with which it is compared also has a deleted relation, and the two relations are different, and by them the thing compared and that with which it is compared differ. The estimation of the speech is: will differs from command, knowledge, and divine approval legally in a difference like the difference that is established rationally. Thus that with which it is compared is the rational distinction, and the thing compared is the legal distinction. Another possibility for the kaaf is that it is a letter indicating causation, and 'maa' is a relative pronoun intended as the proof, and it is as if He said: 'Will differs from command, knowledge, and divine approval because of the proof that is established.'
Source pages p. 245

Commentary
This passage defines the attribute of will (al-irāda) for Allah and proves it from the fact that He acts by choice. The commentator begins by stating that the necessity of this attribute is known necessarily by Ahl al-Sunna due to consensus and scriptural evidence. The proof is: Allah is an agent by choice, and every agent by choice must have will, so Allah has will. Will is then defined as the intention and volition that considers the other option, distinguishing it from mere choice. The commentator then anticipates an objection: if will is defined as considering both sides and inclining to one, then the term 'mushabbah' (the thing compared) and 'mushabbah bihi' (that with which it is compared) would be identical in the simile used in the poem, which is meaningless. The answer clarifies that each has a different implicit relation; in the poem, the comparison is between legal distinction and rational distinction, not between the same thing. Finally, an alternative interpretation of the particle 'ka' is given: it may indicate causation, and 'mā' is the proof, meaning the will differs from command, knowledge, and divine approval because of the proof.

Key Terms
الإرادة
المريد
الاختيار
المشبه
المشبه به
التغاير العقلي
التغاير الشرعي

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The comparison in the poem implies that the legal distinction of will is identical to rational distinction.
Correction: Sanusi clarifies that each has a different implicit relation, so they are distinct.

Checkpoint
What is the proof that Allah has the attribute of will?
A) It is known by revelation alone.
B) Allah is an agent by choice, and every agent by choice has will.
C) The Qur'an uses the word 'will' for Allah.
D) It is a necessary truth independent of reason.

Key Takeaway
Allah's will is proven by His free agency, and careful grammatical analysis resolves apparent contradictions in theological language.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?
The passage engages in meticulous analysis of theological language. ?

The Eternal Attribute of Knowledge
Commentator defines divine knowledge as an eternal attribute.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the definition of Allah's attribute of knowledge as an eternal quality inherent to His essence.

Core Idea
Allah's knowledge is eternal and not acquired.

The commentator begins by affirming that Allah's knowledge is not acquired—it is not something learned or gained over time. This sets the stage for the definition: knowledge is an eternal attribute that subsists by Allah's essence, through which all things are disclosed. This lesson unpacks that definition and its implications.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once, then the translation. Notice the poetic line that introduces the topic and the commentator's expansion.
The passage is incomplete in the text; focus on the key phrase: 'eternal attribute subsisting by His essence'.
2
Understand the negation of acquisition
The passage explicitly says knowledge is 'not said acquired'. Reflect on why this matters: it distinguishes Allah's knowledge from human knowledge, which is acquired through learning.
3
Define the attribute
The definition given: 'an eternal attribute subsisting by His essence'. This tells us knowledge is a real quality of Allah, not a mere concept, and it has no beginning.
The word 'eternal' (azaliyya) means without beginning.

Commentary
This passage presents the definition of the divine attribute of knowledge (al-ʿilm). He defines it as 'an eternal attribute subsisting by His essence, by which is disclosed...' The definition is incomplete in the text, but standard kalam defines it as the attribute by which all things are known. The passage emphasizes that knowledge is not acquired (muktasab) but is inherent and eternal.

The Two Groups That Deny Defining Knowledge
Classification of scholars who deny the definition of knowledge into two subgroups.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the two reasons why some scholars argue that knowledge cannot be defined.

Core Idea
Knowledge is either too obvious or too difficult to define.

Scholars have long debated whether knowledge can be defined. Some say it cannot, but they give different reasons. This lesson introduces those reasons, preparing us to evaluate the arguments on both sides.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage about denial groups
Read the Arabic passage that describes the two groups that deny the definition of knowledge. Notice the structure: first a general statement, then the two subgroups.
The passage uses 'منهم فريق' to introduce each subgroup.
2
Identify the first reason
The first group says knowledge is too obvious to need definition. Understand their analogy: knowledge reveals other things, so it cannot be revealed by something else.
This is a common argument against defining basic concepts.
3
Identify the second reason
The second group says definition is too difficult; every attempted definition has been criticized. Reflect on why a concept might be hard to define.
This group is more pessimistic about the possibility of a successful definition.
4
Connect to the broader debate
Consider how these two positions are different: one says it's unnecessary, the other says it's impossible. Both lead to the same conclusion that knowledge is not defined.
This sets the stage for the next lesson on the opposing view.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أما الكلام على معنى العلم : فاعلم أولا أنَّ العلماء مختلفون في العلم مطلقا، هل يُعرَّف أو لا؟ هذه فمنهم من ذهب إلى أنه لا يعرف ، وهؤلاء انقسموا إلى قسمين: فمنهم فريقٌ ذهب إلى أنَّ علة عدم تعريف العلم: كونه ظاهرا غاية في الظهور، ألا ترى أنه يَكْشِفُ غيرَهُ ، ويوضّحه، فكيف يُظَنُّ أنه في حاجة إلى أن يَكشِفَهُ غيره، وكل شيء غيره ينكشف به ؟ ومنهم فريقٌ ذهب إلى أنَّ علَّة عدم تعريف العلم أنه يعسر تعريفه ، حتى إنه لم يذكر له تعريف إلا وفيه مناقشة وعليه اعتراض. والعلماء من ذهب إلى أنَّ العلم يُعرَّفُ، ولهذا الفريق تعاريف كثيرة للعلم، ومن التعريفات لعلم الله أنه : صفة أزلية قائمة بذاته تعالى متعلقة بجميع الواجبات والجائزات والمستحيلات على وجه الإحاطة على ما هي عليه من غير سبق خفاء
As for discussion of the meaning of knowledge: know first that the scholars differ regarding knowledge absolutely, whether it is defined or not. So among them are those who held that it is not defined, and these are divided into two groups: among them a group held that the reason for not defining knowledge is that it is extremely obvious. Do you not see that it reveals other things and clarifies them, so how can it be thought that it needs something else to reveal it, while everything else is revealed by it? And among them a group held that the reason for not defining knowledge is that its definition is difficult, such that no definition has been mentioned for it except that it contains a flaw and an objection against it. And the scholars who held that knowledge is defined – this group has many definitions for knowledge, and among the definitions for Allah's knowledge is that it is an eternal attribute subsisting in His Essence, related to all obligatory, possible, and impossible things comprehensively as they are, without prior concealment.
Source pages p. 247

Commentary
This passage classifies scholars into three groups regarding the definition of knowledge. The author begins by stating that scholars differ on whether knowledge can be defined. Among those who say it cannot be defined, there are two subgroups: one argues that knowledge is too obvious to need definition—it reveals other things, so how could it need something else to reveal it? The other subgroup argues that definition is too difficult; every attempted definition has been criticized. Then the third group affirms that knowledge can be defined, and they have many definitions. The passage ends by presenting one definition for Allah's knowledge: an eternal attribute subsisting in His Essence, related to all things (obligatory, possible, impossible) comprehensively, without prior concealment. This definition sets the stage for further commentary.

Key Terms
علم
تعريف
ظهور
مناقشة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That all scholars who deny a definition of knowledge agree on the reason.
Correction: The passage shows there are two distinct reasons: one based on obviousness and one based on difficulty.

Checkpoint

A) One
B) Two
C) Three
D) Four

Key Takeaway
Scholars who deny that knowledge can be defined have two distinct reasons: it is either too obvious or too difficult to define.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The first group says knowledge is so obvious it needs no definition. Think of a core belief in your faith that you consider 'obvious.' Have you ever actually studied it deeply, or have you assumed its clarity excuses you from learning? Will you now identify one such belief and seek to understand it more thoroughly?
?
The second group acknowledges the difficulty of defining knowledge, a humbling admission. In what area of your religious practice have you avoided seeking deeper understanding because it seems too complex? What step will you take this week to face that difficulty rather than ignore it?
?
Both groups agree that knowledge cannot be defined, but for opposite reasons. How does this dispute challenge your own tendency to think that every important concept must have a simple, neat definition? How will you respond when you encounter a truth that resists easy explanation?

Affirming the Definition and Defining Divine Knowledge
Presentation of the group that defines knowledge and a definition for Allah's knowledge.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that some scholars do define knowledge, and you will learn one specific definition for Allah's knowledge.

Core Idea
Knowledge can be defined, and Allah's knowledge is an eternal, all-encompassing attribute.

After presenting the arguments against defining knowledge, the author turns to those who affirm definition. They offer many definitions, and one is given here specifically for Allah's knowledge. This lesson presents that definition and its key components.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage about the affirming group
Read the Arabic passage that introduces the scholars who say knowledge can be defined, and the specific definition for Allah's knowledge.
Notice that the definition is detailed and includes multiple qualifiers.
2
Identify the definition's components
The definition says Allah's knowledge is an eternal attribute subsisting in His essence, related to all things (obligatory, possible, impossible) comprehensively, without prior concealment. Break down each part.
Each phrase has theological significance.
3
Understand 'eternal attribute'
This means knowledge is not created or acquired; it has always been an attribute of Allah. It is not a separate entity but subsists in His essence.
This affirms the doctrine of eternal attributes.
4
Understand the scope of divine knowledge
Divine knowledge encompasses all categories: what must happen (obligatory), what may happen (possible), and what cannot happen (impossible). It is comprehensive and without prior ignorance.
This contrasts with human knowledge that is limited and acquired.

Definition of Allah's Knowledge and Its Superiority
The author presents the preferred definition of Allah's knowledge and its components.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the components of the preferred definition of Allah's knowledge and why the author considers it superior.

Core Idea
Allah's knowledge is an eternal attribute encompassing all existence.

The author begins by detailing the definition of Allah's knowledge, explaining each component: 'attribute' as a genus and its specific relation to all possible, necessary, and impossible things. This definition is presented as more appropriate than the alternative offered by the commentator.

Guided Path
1
Read the definition carefully
Read the Arabic passage with translation. Note how 'attribute' functions as a genus and 'related to all...' specifies the unique scope.
2
Identify the components
Recognize that the definition includes a genus (attribute) and a differentia (relation to all things).
The phrase 'pre-eternal determinative knowledge' highlights its timeless nature.
3
Understand the claim of superiority
The author asserts this definition is better than the commentator's, which he will refute next.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقولنا: «صفة» كالجنس في التعريف يشمل كل الصفات، وقولنا : «متعلقة بجميع الواجبات والجائزات والمستحيلات إشارة إلى تعلق العلم التنجيزي القديم وسيأتي بيانه وبيان سائر تعلقاته ، وهذا التعريف أولى من التعريف الذي ذكره الشارح ، فإنَّ تعريفه يَرِدُ عليه اعتراضات كثيرة، منها: أن قوله تنكشف يقتضي بظاهره سبق الخفاء، لأن معنی الانكشاف ظهور الشيء بعد أن لم يكن ظاهراً . ومنها أن قوله: «المعلومات جمع معلومة وهو اسم مفعول من العلم، ومعرفة المشتق متوفقةُ على معرفة أصل الاشتقاق ، فاشتمل التعريف على الدور المحال. ومنها أن قوله: «المعلومات» يقتضي أنها مُنكشفة قبل الانكشاف الحاصل بالعلم، فإنها لا تكون معلومة حتى تكون منكشفة ، ويلزم على هذا: تحصيل الحاصل وهو محال . ومنها قوله: «عند تعلقها بها» يقتضي بظاهره : أنَّ العلم تارة يتعلق بالمعلومات، وتارة لا يتعلق بها وليس كذلك . وعرف بعضهم العلم مطلقا بقوله: «العلم صفةٌ ينكشف بها ما تتعلق به انكشافا لا يحتمل النقيض بوجه من الوجوه، فقوله: «صفة كالجنس في التعريف يشمل جميع الصفات ، وقوله: ينكشف بها ما تتعلق به يخرج الصفات التي لها تعلق ولا تقتضي الانكشاف كالقدرة والإرادة، لأنهما صفتا تأثير كما مرَّ ، ويخرج أيضا الصفات التي لا تتعلق كالحياة
And our saying 'attribute' is like the genus in the definition, encompassing all attributes. And our saying 'related to all obligatory, possible, and impossible things' is an indication of the relation of pre-eternal determinative knowledge, and its explanation and the explanation of its other relations will come. And this definition is more appropriate than the definition mentioned by the commentator, for many objections are raised against his definition, including: that his saying 'it reveals' apparently implies prior concealment, because the meaning of 'revelation' is the appearance of something after it was not apparent. And including: that his saying 'the known things' (al-ma'lumat) is a plural of 'known' which is a passive participle from knowledge, and knowledge of a derivative depends on knowledge of the root derivation, so the definition includes circularity which is impossible. And including: that his saying 'the known things' implies that they are revealed before the revelation by knowledge, for they are not known unless they are revealed, and this requires achieving the already attained, which is impossible. And including: his saying 'when they relate to it' apparently implies that knowledge sometimes relates to the known things and sometimes does not, but it is not so. And some defined knowledge absolutely by saying: 'Knowledge is an attribute by which what it relates to is revealed with a revelation that does not admit contrary in any way.' So his saying 'attribute' like the genus includes all attributes, and his saying 'by which what it relates to is revealed' excludes attributes that have relation but do not necessitate revelation, like power and will, for they are attributes of effect as has passed, and it also excludes attributes that have no relation like life.
Source pages p. 247

Commentary
This passage provides a detailed commentary on the preferred definition of Allah's knowledge and refutes the commentator's alternative definition. The author explains each component of the definition: 'attribute' is a genus covering all attributes; 'related to all obligatory, possible, and impossible things' refers to pre-eternal determinative knowledge. He then states that this definition is superior to the commentator's definition, listing four objections: (1) the commentator's word 'reveals' implies previous concealment, which is inappropriate for Allah; (2) using 'known things' (derived from 'knowledge') leads to circular definition; (3) 'known things' implies they are already revealed before the revelation by knowledge, resulting in 'achieving the already attained'; (4) 'when they relate to it' suggests knowledge sometimes relates and sometimes not, which is false. The passage then presents another definition of knowledge in general (by some scholars): an attribute that reveals its object with certainty, excluding attributes like power and will (which effect but do not reveal) and life (which has no relation). This lesson shows the author's critical approach to definitions.

Key Terms
صفة
متعلقة
تنجيزي

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What does the author say the word 'attribute' does in the definition?
A) It distinguishes divine knowledge from power
B) It serves as a genus covering all attributes
C) It refers only to knowledge
D) It implies temporality

Key Takeaway
The author's definition of Allah's knowledge uses 'attribute' as a genus and specifies its relation to all types of existence, making it more comprehensive than the commentator's.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If Allah's knowledge encompasses every 'possible' and 'impossible' thing, what does that say about your secret doubts and hidden thoughts? Are you living as if He already knows them, or as if you can hide them?
?
The author calls this definition 'more appropriate' than another. When you define Allah in your heart, do you allow any limits or deficiencies? Today, commit to replacing any incomplete concept of Allah with this comprehensive one.
?
You claim to believe in Allah's pre-eternal determinative knowledge. Yet, do you truly entrust every outcome to Him—even the ones you cannot control? Examine one area where you still rely on your own planning instead of His knowledge.

Refutation of the Commentator's Definition of Knowledge
The author refutes the commentator's definition with four objections and provides an alternative definition.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the four logical objections to the commentator's definition of knowledge and the alternative definition that avoids them.

Core Idea
Flawed definitions can imply temporal change, circularity, or redundancy.

The author now launches a sharp critique of the commentator's definition, listing four distinct logical objections. Each objection targets a problematic implication: prior concealment, circular reasoning, attaining the already attained, and suggesting intermittent knowledge. He then presents a sound alternative definition from other scholars.

Guided Path
1
Read the objections
Read the four objections one by one, understanding the logical flaw in each.
The objections are presented as 'منها' (including).
2
Analyze the first two objections
The first objection is about implying prior concealment; the second about circularity due to using a derivative term.
3
Analyze the third and fourth objections
The third objection is about achieving the already attained; the fourth about implying intermittence.
4
Study the alternative definition
Read the definition given by some scholars, noting how it avoids these objections by not using problematic terms.
The author explains how this definition excludes other attributes.

And our saying 'attribute' is like the genus in the definition, encompassing all attributes. And our saying 'related to all obligatory, possible, and impossible things' is an indication of the relation of pre-eternal determinative knowledge, and its explanation and the explanation of its other relations will come. And this definition is more appropriate than the definition mentioned by the commentator, for many objections are raised against his definition, including: that his saying 'it reveals' apparently implies prior concealment, because the meaning of 'revelation' is the appearance of something after it was not apparent. And including: that his saying 'the known things' (al-ma'lumat) is a plural of 'known' which is a passive participle from knowledge, and knowledge of a derivative depends on knowledge of the root derivation, so the definition includes circularity which is impossible. And including: that his saying 'the known things' implies that they are revealed before the revelation by knowledge, for they are not known unless they are revealed, and this requires achieving the already attained, which is impossible. And including: his saying 'when they relate to it' apparently implies that knowledge sometimes relates to the known things and sometimes does not, but it is not so. And some defined knowledge absolutely by saying: 'Knowledge is an attribute by which what it relates to is revealed with a revelation that does not admit contrary in any way.' So his saying 'attribute' like the genus includes all attributes, and his saying 'by which what it relates to is revealed' excludes attributes that have relation but do not necessitate revelation, like power and will, for they are attributes of effect as has passed, and it also excludes attributes that have no relation like life.
Source pages p. 247

Commentary
This passage provides a detailed commentary on the preferred definition of Allah's knowledge and refutes the commentator's alternative definition. The author explains each component of the definition: 'attribute' is a genus covering all attributes; 'related to all obligatory, possible, and impossible things' refers to pre-eternal determinative knowledge. He then states that this definition is superior to the commentator's definition, listing four objections: (1) the commentator's word 'reveals' implies previous concealment, which is inappropriate for Allah; (2) using 'known things' (derived from 'knowledge') leads to circular definition; (3) 'known things' implies they are already revealed before the revelation by knowledge, resulting in 'achieving the already attained'; (4) 'when they relate to it' suggests knowledge sometimes relates and sometimes not, which is false. The passage then presents another definition of knowledge in general (by some scholars): an attribute that reveals its object with certainty, excluding attributes like power and will (which effect but do not reveal) and life (which has no relation). This lesson shows the author's critical approach to definitions.

Key Terms
انكشاف
دور
تحصيل الحاصل
معلوم
عند تعلقها

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think the commentator's definition is acceptable because it describes revelation.
Correction: The author shows that the wording implies temporal change and logical circularity, making it unsuitable for eternal divine knowledge.

Checkpoint

A) Prior concealment
B) Circularity
C) Achieving the already attained
D) Intermittent relation

Key Takeaway
The commentator's definition fails due to implications of temporality, circularity, redundancy, and intermittence; a sound definition must avoid these pitfalls.

?

The Preferred Definition of God's Knowledge
The author gives the preferred definition of God's knowledge and explains its components.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the preferred definition of God's knowledge in Ash'ari theology, including its key components and why it is considered superior.

Core Idea
God's knowledge is an eternal, comprehensive attribute that knows all things exactly as they are.

In this passage, the author presents a specific definition of God's knowledge that he considers superior. This definition grounds God's knowledge in eternity and comprehensiveness, highlighting that it encompasses all categories of existence: necessary, possible, and impossible. Understanding this definition is crucial for grasping the Islamic conception of divine omniscience.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage carefully, then its literal translation. Notice the structure of the definition and the subsequent explanation.
2
Identify the key components
The definition has four parts: 'attribute', 'eternal', 'subsisting in His essence', and 'related to all necessary, possible, and impossible things'. Note how each part adds a specific meaning.
3
Understand the significance of 'related to all...'
The phrase 'related to all necessary, possible, and impossible things' indicates the comprehensiveness of God's knowledge and points to the concept of 'eternal actual knowledge' which will be explained later.
4
Compare with the commentator's definition
The author states that this definition is more appropriate than the one given by the commentator. Reflect on why this might be so based on the definition's precision.
5
Address a common misunderstanding
One might think God's knowledge is like human knowledge. This definition counters that by emphasizing eternity and comprehensiveness, showing it is unlike created knowledge.

⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ومن العلماء من ذهب إلى أنَّ العلم يُعرَّفُ، ولهذا الفريق تعاريف كثيرة للعلم، ومن التعريفات لعلم الله أنه : صفة أزلية قائمة بذاته تعالى متعلقة بجميع الواجبات والجائزات والمستحيلات على وجه الإحاطة على ما هي عليه من غير سبق خفاء» وقولنا: «صفة» كالجنس في التعريف يشمل كل الصفات، وقولنا : «متعلقة بجميع الواجبات والجائزات والمستحيلات إشارة إلى تعلق العلم التنجيزي القديم وسيأتي بيانه وبيان سائر تعلقاته ، وهذا التعريف أولى من التعريف الذي ذكره الشارح ،
And among the scholars are those who hold that knowledge is defined, and this group has many definitions for knowledge. Among the definitions of God's knowledge is that it is: an eternal attribute subsisting in His essence, related to all necessary things, possible things, and impossible things in a comprehensive manner as they are, without prior concealment. And our saying 'attribute' is like the genus in the definition, encompassing all attributes. And our saying 'related to all necessary, possible, and impossible things' indicates the relation of eternal actual knowledge, and its explanation and the explanation of its other relations will come. And this definition is more appropriate than the definition mentioned by the commentator.
Source pages p. 247

Commentary
This passage presents a specific definition of God's knowledge preferred by the author. After noting that some scholars do define knowledge, the author selects a definition that describes God's knowledge as 'an eternal attribute subsisting in His essence' (صفة أزلية قائمة بذاته). This definition highlights that God's knowledge is not acquired but is an essential attribute. The definition states that this knowledge is related to all necessary things (واجبات), possible things (جائزات), and impossible things (مستحيلات) in a comprehensive manner. The phrase 'as they are' (على ما هي عليه) emphasizes that God knows things exactly as they are, and 'without prior concealment' (من غير سبق خفاء) refutes any notion of prior ignorance. The author then explains that the word 'attribute' (صفة) serves as a genus covering all attributes, and the phrase 'related to all...' specifically points to the eternal actual relation (تعلق التنجيزي القديم) of God's knowledge, which will be elaborated later. The author concludes that this definition is superior to the one given by the commentator (الشارح).

The Types of Relation of God's Knowledge
The author presents the majority and minority views on the relations of God's knowledge.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the two main positions on how God's eternal knowledge relates to temporal objects, and the arguments each side uses.

Core Idea
God's knowledge relates to everything in one eternal act; some say there are three types of relation.

This passage presents a major debate in Islamic theology: does God's knowledge have a single unchanging relation to all things, or does it have multiple relations depending on their existence? The majority of kalam scholars argue for a single eternal actual relation, while a minority propose three distinct relations. Understanding this debate clarifies how divine omniscience is reconciled with temporal change.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis, then the translation. Let the author's structure come through.
Notice the author presents two views: 'جمهرة' (majority) and 'قوم' (a group).
2
Identify the majority view
Find the majority's claim: one eternal actual relation (تعلق تنجيزي قديم). Note their denial of potential and temporal actual relations.
Their reasoning: potential knowledge is not actual, and temporal actual implies prior ignorance.
3
Identify the minority view
Find the three relations: eternal actual (to God's essence/attributes), eternal potential (to other things before existence), and temporal actual (after existence).
Minority defends potential relation as not implying ignorance.
4
Analyze the minority's response
Examine how they rebut the majority's argument: non-relation does not equal ignorance, just as inability for the impossible is not weakness.
This shows the nuance in their position.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما القول في تعلقات العلم، فإنا نقول: ذهب جمهرة علماء الكلام إلى أنَّ لعلم الله تعالى تعلقا تنجيزيا قديما بجميع الأشياء؛ فهو سبحانه يعلم الأشياء في الأزل على ما هي عليه ، فأما كونها وُجدت في الماضي أو موجودةً في الحال أو توجد في المستقبل فأطوار في المعلومات لا توجب تغيرا في تعلّق العلم، فالمتغيّر إنَّما هو صفة المعلوم، لا تعلق العلم، وهؤلاء الجمهرة من العلماء ينفون أن يكون لعلم الله تعالى تعلّق صلوحي بالأشياء، كما ينفون أن يكون له بها تعلق تنجيزي حادث ، قالوا: لأن الصالح لأن يعلم ليس بعالم فعلا، والتنجيزي الحادث يستلزم سبق الجهل، وكلاهما لا يجوز في حقه تعالى ، وذهب قوم إلى إثبات ثلاث تعلقات لعلم الله تعالى ، أولها التنجيزي القديم بالنسبة لذات الله تعالى وصفاته وثانيها: الصلوحي القديم بالنسبة لغيره سبحانه قبل وجود ذلك الغير، قالوا: العلم صالح لأن يتعلق بوجود الشيء قبل وجوده وهو حينئذ غير متعلق بوجوده، لأن العلم بوجود الشيء قبل وجوده جهل، نعم العلم بأن غير الموجود سيكون وسيوجد تنجيزي قديم، وأجاب عن قول الأولين: «إن الصالح لأن يعلم ليس بعالم فعلا»، بأن ثبوت الوجود لزيد بالفعل لا يصلح أن يكون معلوماً قبل وجوده بالفعل، وعدم تعلق العلم بشيء لا يصلح أن يكون معلوماً لا يعد جهلا ، كما أن عدم القدرة على المستحيل لا يعد عجزاً، وثالثها: تعلق تنجيزي حادث بالنسبة لغيره تعالى بعد وجوده بالفعل.
As for the discussion on the relations of knowledge, we say: the majority of the scholars of kalam hold that God's knowledge has an eternal actual relation to all things; for He, exalted, knows things in eternity as they are. As for their being in the past, present, or future, these are states in the known things that do not necessitate a change in the relation of knowledge. The changing is only the attribute of the known, not the relation of knowledge. And these majority of scholars deny that God's knowledge has a potential relation to things, just as they deny that it has a temporal actual relation. They said: because that which is fit to know is not actually knowing, and the temporal actual relation entails prior ignorance, and both are not permissible for Him, exalted. And a group went to affirming three relations of God's knowledge: first, the eternal actual relation with respect to God's essence and attributes; second, the eternal potential relation with respect to other than Him before the existence of that other. They said: knowledge is fit to be related to the existence of a thing before its existence, and it is then not related to its existence, because knowledge of the existence of a thing before its existence is ignorance. Yes, knowledge that the non-existent will be and will exist is eternal actual. And they responded to the saying of the first group, 'that which is fit to know is not actually knowing', by saying that the actual existence of Zayd is not fit to be known before its actual existence, and non-relation of knowledge to a thing does not count as ignorance, just as inability regarding the impossible does not count as incapacity. And third: a temporal actual relation with respect to other than Him after its actual existence.
Source pages p. 248

Commentary
This passage discusses the types of relation (تعلق) of God's knowledge to objects. The author first presents the majority view (جمهرة علماء الكلام): that God's knowledge has a single eternal actual relation (تعلق تنجيزي قديم) to all things. This means God knows everything in eternity as it is; past, present, and future are just modes of the known objects and do not change the relation of knowledge. The majority denies both a potential relation (تعلق صلوحي) and a temporal actual relation (تعلق تنجيزي حادث). Their reasoning is that potential knowledge does not constitute actual knowledge, and a temporal actual relation would imply prior ignorance, which is impossible for God. The author then presents a minority view that affirms three relations: (1) eternal actual relation to God's own essence and attributes; (2) eternal potential relation to other things before their existence, arguing that knowledge can be 'fit' to relate to a thing's existence before it exists without actually relating to it (since that would be ignorance); they give the example that knowledge that something will exist is actual, not potential; (3) a temporal actual relation after the thing's existence. The minority responds to the majority's objection by arguing that non-relation of knowledge to a thing does not entail ignorance, just as inability regarding the impossible is not weakness. This passage thus presents a nuanced debate on the nature of divine omniscience.

Key Terms
تعلق تنجيزي
تعلق صلوحي
تنجيزي حادث
تنجيزي قديم

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: God's knowledge changes as events happen, having different relations to past, present, and future.
Correction: The majority holds that God's knowledge has a single eternal actual relation unaffected by temporal modes; the minority adds a potential relation before existence but still maintains eternal knowledge of future events.

Checkpoint
What is the majority view on the relation of God's knowledge to temporal events?
A) It has a temporal actual relation for future events.
B) It has a single eternal actual relation that does not change.
C) It has multiple relations depending on the event.
D) It has no relation to contingent things.

Key Takeaway
The debate centers on whether divine knowledge adapts to temporal existence or remains eternally fixed in its relation; both views affirm omniscience but differ on how relation is conceived.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you plan your future, do you act as if God is learning along with you, or do you truly believe His knowledge of every detail is eternally settled and perfect? How would your anxiety change if you lived each moment trusting that God already knows exactly what will happen and has arranged it wisely? Will you consciously remind yourself of His eternal knowledge whenever worry overtakes you?
?
The minority view says God's knowledge has a potential relation to things before they exist — He knows they are fit to exist. How does this affect your view of God's care for you even before you were born? Are you living with the humility that your entire life, every joy and trial, was fully present in God's eternal knowledge? Will you thank Him today for knowing you before you existed?
?
The majority insists that God's relation to all things is eternal and unchanging. But do you sometimes wish God's knowledge would adapt to your changing circumstances — that He might change His mind about your situation? This lesson confronts you: will you submit to a God whose knowledge is fixed and unalterable, even when it feels uncomfortable? What practical steps can you take to embrace His unchanging knowledge instead of resisting it?

Knowledge as Certainty, Correspondence, and Stability
Definition of knowledge and enumeration of its three necessary aspects.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the definition of knowledge in Islamic theology and its three necessary aspects: certainty, correspondence with reality, and stability.

Core Idea
True knowledge must be certain, correspond to reality, and be stable.

In this passage, the author defines the attribute of knowledge for God and clarifies what sets it apart from other mental states like doubt and false belief. He draws out three essential aspects that every instance of knowledge must have. Understanding these aspects is crucial for distinguishing genuine knowledge from mere opinion or guesswork.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage
Read the Arabic passage once without stopping. Then read the translation. Notice how the author begins with a general definition and then specifies it.
2
Identify the three necessary aspects
The passage lists three things that knowledge necessitates: certainty, correspondence, and stability. Find each term in the Arabic and note the explanation that follows.
These three aspects are derived from the phrase 'does not admit the opposite in any manner.'
3
Connect aspects to the three domains
The author explains that non-admission of the opposite applies mentally (due to certainty), externally (due to correspondence), and against a doubter (due to stability). Reflect on how each aspect rules out a specific kind of opposite-admission.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
صفة العلم، تعريفها، ودليل ثبوتها له تعالى والمراد بالانكشاف ما هو أعم من الانكشاف التّامّ، ولهذا أتى بعده بقوله: «لا يحتمل النقيض» لأجل إخراج الظن، والوهم، والشك، والاعتقاد الجازم، سواء أكان مطابقا للواقع أم لم يكن مطابقا، لأن متعلقات هذه الأشياء تحتمل النقيض، وقوله: «بوجه من الوجوه» أراد به: ألا يحتمل النقيض بحسب الدهن، ولا بحسب الخارج، ولا لأجل تشكيك مشكك، وأشار به إلى أن العلم تلزمه أمور ثلاثة: الجزم، والمطابقة للواقع، والثبات على ما علمه، فالعلم بالشيء لا بد أن يكون جازما به وثابتا عليه، ولا بد أن يكون معلومه مطابقا للواقع، فعدم احتمال معلومه للنقيض بحسب الذهن لأنه جازم به وعدم احتمال معلومه للنقيض بحسب الخارج لأنه مطابق للواقع، وعدم احتمال معلومه النقيض بسبب تشكيك المشكك؛ لأنه ثابت عليه، وفي هذا القدر كفاية.
The attribute of knowledge, its definition, and the proof of its establishment for Him, may He be exalted. And what is meant by 'disclosure' is more general than complete disclosure, therefore he followed it with his statement: 'does not admit the opposite' in order to exclude supposition, illusion, doubt, and firm belief, whether it corresponds to reality or does not correspond, because the objects of these things admit the opposite. And his statement: 'in any manner' intended by it: that it does not admit the opposite mentally, nor externally, nor due to the doubt of a doubter. And he indicated by it that knowledge necessitates three things: certainty, correspondence with reality, and stability upon what one knows. So knowledge of something must be certain about it and stable upon it, and its object must correspond to reality. So the non-admission of its object to the opposite mentally is because he is certain about it, and the non-admission of its object to the opposite externally is because it corresponds to reality, and the non-admission of its object to the opposite due to the doubt of a doubter is because he is stable upon it. And in this amount is sufficient.
Source pages p. 248

Commentary
This passage defines the attribute of knowledge (العلم) for God. The author first states that knowledge is complete disclosure that does not admit the opposite. He explains that 'disclosure' is general, so he specifies it with 'does not admit the opposite' to exclude other mental states like opinion, illusion, doubt, and even firm belief that might not correspond to reality. Then he explains the phrase 'in any manner' to emphasize that the object of knowledge must not admit the opposite mentally, externally, or due to a doubter. He then derives three necessary aspects of knowledge: certainty (الجزم), correspondence with reality (المطابقة للواقع), and stability (الثبات). The non-admission of the opposite by the mind is due to certainty, by external reality due to correspondence, and by the doubt of a doubter due to stability. This definition is crucial for distinguishing true knowledge from other states.

Key Terms
علم
انكشاف
جزم
مطابقة
ثبات

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That knowledge includes any firm belief, even if false.
Correction: Knowledge requires correspondence with reality; a false conviction is not knowledge.

Checkpoint
What are the three necessary aspects of knowledge according to this passage?
A) Certainty, correspondence, and stability
B) Certainty, doubt, and stability
C) Opinion, correspondence, and stability
D) Certainty, correspondence, and change

Key Takeaway
Knowledge is distinguished by three inseparable features: certainty, correspondence to reality, and stability.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

Debate on Divine Knowledge Relations
The author presents the debate between the majority and a group over the types of divine knowledge.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the debate between the majority and a minority group over the number and type of relations of God's knowledge.

Core Idea
God's knowledge is timeless; the debate is about how it relates to temporal things.

This passage presents a central dispute in kalam about the nature of divine omniscience. The majority holds that God's knowledge has a single eternal actual relation to all things, while a minority argues for three distinct relations. Understanding this debate clarifies how God can know temporal events without change or ignorance.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic and translation to understand the two positions: the majority view and the minority view.
Pay attention to the terms 'تنجيزي' (actual) and 'صلوحي' (potential).
2
Compare the two positions
Notice that the majority denies both potential and temporal actual relations, while the minority affirms three relations. Ask yourself why each group argues as they do.
3
Examine the objection and answer
The majority objects that 'that which is fit to know is not actually knowing'. The answer clarifies that not knowing what is not fit to be known is not ignorance. Reflect on the analogy with the impossible and incapacity.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما القول في تعلقات العلم، فإنا نقول: ذهب جمهرة علماء الكلام إلى أنَّ لعلم الله تعالى تعلقا تنجيزيا قديما الأشياء؛ فهو سبحانه يعلم الأشياء في الأزل على ما بجميع هي عليه، فأما كونها وُجدت في الماضي أو موجودةً في الحال أو توجد في المستقبل فأطوار في المعلومات لا توجب تغيرا في تعلّق العلم، فالمتغيّر إنَّما هو صفة المعلوم، لا تعلق العلم، وهؤلاء الجمهرة من العلماء ينفون أن يكون لعلم الله تعالى تعلّق صلوحي بالأشياء، كما ينفون أن يكون له بها تعلق تنجيزي حادث، قالوا: لأن الصالح لأن يعلم ليس بعالم فعلا، والتنجيزي الحادث يستلزم سبق الجهل، وكلاهما لا يجوز في حقه تعالى، وذهب قوم إلى إثبات ثلاث تعلقات لعلم الله تعالى، أولها التنجيزي القديم بالنسبة لذات الله تعالى وصفاته وثانيها: الصلوحي القديم بالنسبة لغيره سبحانه قبل وجود ذلك الغير، قالوا: العلم صالح لأن يتعلق بوجود الشيء قبل وجوده وهو حينئذ غير متعلق بوجوده، لأن العلم بوجود الشيء قبل وجوده جهل، نعم العلم بأن غير الموجود سيكون وسيوجد تنجيزي قديم، وأجاب ء عن قول الأولين: «إن الصالح لأن يعلم ليس بعالم فعلا»، بأن ثبوت الوجود لزيد بالفعل لا يصلح أن يكون معلوماً قبل وجوده بالفعل، وعدم تعلق العلم بشيء لا يصلح أن يكون معلوماً قبل وجوده بالفعل وعدم تعلق العلم بشيء لا يصلح أن يكون معلوماً لا يعد جهلا، كما أن عدم القدرة على المستحيل لا يعد عجزاً، وثالثها: تعلق تنجيزي حادث بالنسبة لغيره تعالى بعد وجوده بالفعل.
As for the discussion on the relations of knowledge, we say: The majority of kalam scholars held that God's knowledge has one eternal actual relation to things; He, exalted, knows things from eternity as they are. As for their having existed in the past, existing in the present, or existing in the future, these are modes in the known things that do not cause a change in the relation of knowledge. The changing is only the attribute of the known, not the relation of knowledge. And this majority of scholars deny that God's knowledge has a potential relation to things, just as they deny that it has a temporal actual relation to them. They said: because that which is fit to know is not actually knowing, and the temporal actual entails prior ignorance, and both are not permissible for Him, exalted. And a group went to affirming three relations of God's knowledge: the first is the eternal actual relation regarding God's essence and attributes; the second is the eternal potential relation regarding other than Him, exalted, before the existence of that other. They said: knowledge is fit to be related to the existence of a thing before its existence, and it is then not related to its existence, because knowledge of the existence of a thing before its existence is ignorance. Yes, knowledge that the non-existent will be and will exist is eternal actual. And he answered regarding the statement of the first group: 'That which is fit to know is not actually knowing' — that the actual existence of Zayd is not fit to be known before its actual existence. And the non-relation of knowledge to something that is not fit to be known is not considered ignorance, just as the inability with respect to the impossible is not considered incapacity. And the third: a temporal actual relation regarding other than Him, exalted, after its actual existence.
Source pages p. 248

Commentary
This passage presents a theological debate about the relation (تعلق) of God's knowledge to objects. The majority of kalam scholars hold that God's knowledge has a single eternal actual relation to all things; He knows them from eternity as they are, regardless of their temporal modes (past, present, future). They deny both a potential relation (which would imply that He is not actually knowing at the moment) and a temporal actual relation (which would imply prior ignorance). A group, however, posits three relations: an eternal actual relation to God's essence and attributes, an eternal potential relation to other things before their existence (since knowledge of them before existence would be ignorance if it were actual), and a temporal actual relation after their existence. The author presents an objection from the majority that 'that which is fit to know is not actually knowing' and supplies an answer: the actual existence of a thing is not fit to be known before it exists, and not knowing what is not fit to be known is not ignorance, just as inability regarding the impossible is not impotence. This is a classic kalam debate about how God's timeless knowledge relates to temporal events.

Key Terms
تعلق
تنجيزي
صلوحي
قديم
حادث

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: God's knowledge changes as things come into existence.
Correction: The passage argues that change is in the objects, not in the knowledge relation. The majority says knowledge relation is unchanging; the minority says relations differ but knowledge itself is eternal.

Checkpoint
According to the majority view, what kind of relation does God's knowledge have to things?
A) One eternal actual relation
B) Three relations: eternal actual, eternal potential, temporal actual
C) Only a temporal actual relation
D) Only a potential relation

Key Takeaway
The majority of kalam scholars maintain that God's knowledge has one unchanging eternal actual relation to all things, while a minority argues for three relations to accommodate temporal existence without implying ignorance or change in God.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Your past sins and future actions are known to God eternally. Does it comfort you or trouble you to think that God's knowledge relates to them with a single unchanging relation? How does that affect your motivation to repent or strive?
?
The minority view says God's knowledge has a temporal actual relation to your deeds after you do them. Does that make you feel more personally accountable for each moment? Why does it matter whether God 'knows' your action at the moment you do it or from eternity?
?
If God's inability to know a non-existent thing before it exists is not a deficiency, what does that say about your own limitations in understanding God's perfection? How can you humble yourself before this mystery rather than try to fully grasp it?

Proofs of God's Knowledge
Sanusi gives two proofs for God's knowledge.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand two rational proofs that establish God's knowledge of all things: the perfection of creation and the necessity of intending.

Core Idea
God's perfect work and purposeful action prove His all-encompassing knowledge.

Before we delve into the nature of God's knowledge, the author first establishes that God indeed knows all things. He provides two clear proofs: one from the quality of creation, the other from the nature of intentional action. This lesson walks through these arguments.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once to get the overall argument. Note the two proofs mentioned.
The passage begins by stating that everything possible is known to God.
2
Understand the first proof
The first proof is from the perfection of creation (itqan). God’s act is perfectly made and firm; such an act can only come from a knowing agent.
This is a common kalam argument: perfect design implies a knowing designer.
3
Understand the second proof
The second proof is from purposeful action (qasd). God acts with intention and choice, which is impossible without knowledge of the intended object.
This refutes any notion of an ignorant creator.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قسم الإلهيات بها المعلومات (١) عند تعلقها بها ، وجميع ما يمكن أن يتعلق به العلم فهو معلوم له تعالى. لأنَّه فاعل فعلاً مُتقناً محكماً، وكلُّ مَن كان كذلك فهو عالِمٌ (٢) ، ولأنَّه تعالى فاعل بالقصد والاختيار، ولا يُتصور ذلك إلا مع العلم بالمقصودِ، لاستحالة توجهِ القصدِ والإرادةِ مِن الفاعل إلى ما لم يعلم، وهو أقوى في = والصحيح الذي ينبغي أن تأخذ به هو: : أن للعلم تعلقاً واحداً ، هو التنجيزي القديم، وهو ما نسبناه للجمهرة من علماء ،الكلام ومولانا الله يعلم الأشياء إجمالا وتفصيلا، وهو عالم بالكليات والجزئيات، ويعلم ما لا نهاية له ككمالاته وأنفاس أهل الجنة يعلمها تفصيلا ويعلم أنها لا تتناهى . وأما ادعاء أن العلم التفصيلي يتوقف على كون الشيء متناهيا فما لا يتناهى لا تمكن معرفته تفصيلا ، فهذا بحسب عقولنا.
Section on Divine Matters: By it the known things at their relation to it, and everything that can be related to knowledge is known to Him, exalted. Because He does a perfect, firm act, and whoever is like that is knowing. And because He, exalted, acts with intention and choice, and that is not conceivable except with knowledge of the intended object, due to the impossibility of directing intention and will from the agent to what he does not know. And this is stronger... and the correct view that you should adopt is that knowledge has one relation, which is the eternal actual, which we have attributed to the majority of kalam scholars. And our Lord, God, knows things collectively and in detail, and He is knowing of universals and particulars, and He knows what has no end, such as His perfections and the breaths of the people of Paradise; He knows them in detail and knows that they are infinite. As for the claim that detailed knowledge depends on the thing being finite, and that what is infinite cannot be known in detail, this is according to our intellects.
Source pages p. 249

Commentary
This passage provides two proofs for God's knowledge of all things and declares the correct view. First, by the perfection of creation (الاتقان): God's act is perfectly made and firm, and anyone who produces such an act must have knowledge. Second, by purposefulness: God acts with intention and choice, which is impossible without knowledge of the intended object. Then the author affirms that the correct view is the one held by the majority: God's knowledge is a single eternal actual relation. He emphasizes that God knows things both collectively and in detail, including universals and particulars, and even infinite things like His own perfections and the unlimited breaths of Paradise. He refutes the claim that detailed knowledge requires finitude, stating that such limitation is only relevant to human intellects.

D) Potentiality of knowledge and actuality of existence

Key Takeaway
God's knowledge is proven by the perfection of His creation and His intentional action.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If God's perfect act of creation demonstrates His detailed knowledge, then your personal struggles are not unknown to Him. What specific area of your life do you need to trust that God fully understands today? Take a moment to entrust it to Him.
?
You act with intention because you know what you want. God's intentional actions prove He knows you intimately. Are you living as if God truly knows your heart, or do you hide parts of your life thinking He doesn't see? What will you bring into the light this week?
?
The proof from intention shows that God's will is directed toward what He knows. Your decisions matter because they are known. How does knowing that God sees every choice you make change how you will approach your next decision? Commit to one area where you will seek His guidance.

One Eternal Actual Knowledge
Sanusi affirms one eternal actual knowledge and refutes finitude objection.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that God's knowledge is a single eternal actual relation encompassing all things, including infinite particulars.

Core Idea
God's knowledge is one, eternal, and encompasses all, even the infinite.

After establishing that God knows all things, the author turns to the nature of that knowledge. He affirms the majority view that God's knowledge is a single eternal actual relation. He also addresses a potential objection about knowing infinite details.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and identify the correct view
The author declares that the correct view is that knowledge has one relation, the eternal actual (al-tunjizi al-qadim). This is the position of the majority of kalam scholars.
2
Consider the scope of God's knowledge
God knows things collectively and in detail, universals and particulars, and even infinite things such as His perfections and the breaths of the people of Paradise.
This refutes any limitation on God's knowledge.
3
Understand the refutation of the objection
Some claim that detailed knowledge requires the object to be finite, so infinite things cannot be known in detail. The author responds that this is a limitation of human intellects, not God's.
This is a key point: God's knowledge is not bound by human constraints.

Proofs of God's Comprehensive Knowledge
Proofs that God knows all things and explanation of His all-encompassing knowledge.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the rational proofs that God knows all things, including the infinite, and the correct theological view of divine knowledge as one eternal attribute encompassing all objects.

Core Idea
God's knowledge is one eternal attribute encompassing all things in general and detail.

This lesson explores the arguments that God knows everything—from universals to infinite particulars. It establishes that divine knowledge is not limited by human intellectual constraints and clarifies the nature of this attribute.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once to absorb the flow. Then read the translation to understand the meaning.
Notice the concise style of theological argumentation.
2
Identify the two proofs
Look for the two distinct arguments: one from the perfection of God's action, the other from His purposeful agency.
Each proof leads to the conclusion that God must be knowing.
3
Understand the nature of divine knowledge
The passage explains that God's knowledge is one pre-eternal actual relationship that encompasses all things, including the infinite, in general and detail.
This counters the idea that detailed knowledge requires finiteness.
4
Reflect on the implication
Consider why the claim that infinite things cannot be known in detail is a limitation of human intellect, not of God.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قسم الإلهيات بها المعلومات عند تعلقها بها، وجميع ما يمكن أن يتعلق به العلم فهو معلوم له تعالى. لأنه فاعل فعلا متقنا محكما، وكل من كان كذلك فهو عالم، ولأنه تعالى فاعل بالقصد والاختيار، ولا يتصور ذلك إلا مع العلم بالمقصود، لاستحالة توجه القصد والإرادة من الفاعل إلى ما لم يعلم. والصحيح الذي ينبغي أن تأخذ به هو أن للعلم تعلقا واحدا، هو التنجيزي القديم، وهو ما نسبناه للجمهرة من علماء الكلام، ومولانا الله يعلم الأشياء إجمالا وتفصيلا، وهو عالم بالكليات والجزئيات، ويعلم ما لا نهاية له ككمالاته وأنفاس أهل الجنة يعلمها تفصيلا ويعلم أنها لا تتناهى. وأما ادعاء أن العلم التفصيلي يتوقف على كون الشيء متناهيا فما لا يتناهى لا تمكن معرفته تفصيلا، فهذا بحسب عقولنا. ويدخل فيما يتعلق به العلم علمه تعالى، فهو سبحانه يعلم أن له علما. وذهبت الفلاسفة إلى أنه سبحانه لا يعلم الجزئيات، وهو أحد مسائل ثلاث كفروا بها، وثانيها ادعاؤهم قدم العالم، وثالثها إنكار بعث الأجساد وادعاؤهم أن الذي يحشر هو الأرواح، وقد نظم هذه المسائل بعضهم في قوله: بثلاثة كفر الفلاسفة العدى إذ أنكروها وهي علم بجزئي، حدوث عوالم حشر لأجساد وكانت ميتة. اهـ محي.
Section on Divine Matters: All known things are known by God when His knowledge relates to them, and everything that can be known is known to Him. Because He is an agent who performs an action precisely and perfectly, and everyone who does so is knowing; and because He is an agent with purpose and choice, and that cannot be conceived except with knowledge of the intended object, due to the impossibility of the agent directing intent and will toward what he does not know. The correct view that you should adopt is that knowledge has one relationship, which is the pre-eternal actual relationship, which we attribute to the majority of theologians. And our Lord God knows things in general and in detail, and He is knowing of universals and particulars, and He knows what has no end, such as His perfections and the breaths of the people of Paradise; He knows them in detail and knows that they are infinite. As for the claim that detailed knowledge depends on the thing being finite, and that what is infinite cannot be known in detail, this is relative to our intellects. His knowledge of Himself is included in what His knowledge relates to, for He, exalted, knows that He has knowledge. The philosophers held that He does not know particulars, and this is one of three issues for which they were deemed disbelievers: the second is their claim of the eternity of the world, and the third is their denial of the resurrection of bodies and their claim that what is resurrected is the souls. Some of them versified these issues in his saying: 'By three things the hostile philosophers disbelieved when they denied them: knowledge of a particular, origination of worlds, resurrection of bodies that were dead.' End of al-Mahi.
Source pages p. 249

Commentary
This passage presents proofs that God knows all things, including particulars, and refutes the philosophers who deny His knowledge of particulars. It begins by stating that every possible object of knowledge is known to God, then gives two arguments: first, God acts with flawless precision, and whoever does so must be knowing; second, God acts with purpose and choice, which requires knowledge of the intended object. It then clarifies the nature of divine knowledge: it is one eternal attribute encompassing all things in general and in detail, including infinite things like God's perfections and the infinite breaths of Paradise. The claim that detailed knowledge requires finitude is rejected as a limitation of human intellect. The passage then addresses the philosophers' denial of God's knowledge of particulars, listing it as the first of three issues for which they were deemed disbelievers, the others being the eternity of the world and denial of bodily resurrection. A poetic summary of these issues is provided.

Refuting the Philosophers on Knowledge of Particulars
Refutation of philosophers who deny God's knowledge of particulars and statement of their three errors.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the refutation of the philosophical claim that God does not know particulars, and the three major errors that led to their classification as disbelievers.

Core Idea
Denying God's knowledge of particulars is one of three grave errors that exclude one from Islam.

This lesson addresses the philosophers' denial of God's knowledge of particulars and presents the orthodox response. It also outlines the three theological errors—denial of knowledge of particulars, eternity of the world, and denial of bodily resurrection—that render them disbelievers.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Notice how it moves from self-knowledge to the philosophers' position.
2
Understand the refutation
The passage includes God's knowledge of Himself as part of His comprehensive knowledge, then refutes the claim that detailed knowledge requires finitude.
The refutation is implicit: God's knowledge is not bound by human limitations.
3
Identify the three errors
List the three issues: denial of knowledge of particulars, eternity of the world, and denial of bodily resurrection.
These are considered causes of disbelief (kufr).
4
Reflect on the poetic summary
Read the versification that summarizes the three issues. This helps in memorization.

قسم الإلهيات بها المعلومات عند تعلقها بها، وجميع ما يمكن أن يتعلق به العلم فهو معلوم له تعالى. لأنه فاعل فعلا متقنا محكما، وكل من كان كذلك فهو عالم، ولأنه تعالى فاعل بالقصد والاختيار، ولا يتصور ذلك إلا مع العلم بالمقصود، لاستحالة توجه القصد والإرادة من الفاعل إلى ما لم يعلم. والصحيح الذي ينبغي أن تأخذ به هو أن للعلم تعلقا واحدا، هو التنجيزي القديم، وهو ما نسبناه للجمهرة من علماء الكلام، ومولانا الله يعلم الأشياء إجمالا وتفصيلا، وهو عالم بالكليات والجزئيات، ويعلم ما لا نهاية له ككمالاته وأنفاس أهل الجنة يعلمها تفصيلا ويعلم أنها لا تتناهى. وأما ادعاء أن العلم التفصيلي يتوقف على كون الشيء متناهيا فما لا يتناهى لا تمكن معرفته تفصيلا، فهذا بحسب عقولنا. ويدخل فيما يتعلق به العلم علمه تعالى، فهو سبحانه يعلم أن له علما. وذهبت الفلاسفة إلى أنه سبحانه لا يعلم الجزئيات، وهو أحد مسائل ثلاث كفروا بها، وثانيها ادعاؤهم قدم العالم، وثالثها إنكار بعث الأجساد وادعاؤهم أن الذي يحشر هو الأرواح، وقد نظم هذه المسائل بعضهم في قوله: بثلاثة كفر الفلاسفة العدى إذ أنكروها وهي علم بجزئي، حدوث عوالم حشر لأجساد وكانت ميتة. اهـ محي.
Section on Divine Matters: All known things are known by God when His knowledge relates to them, and everything that can be known is known to Him. Because He is an agent who performs an action precisely and perfectly, and everyone who does so is knowing; and because He is an agent with purpose and choice, and that cannot be conceived except with knowledge of the intended object, due to the impossibility of the agent directing intent and will toward what he does not know. The correct view that you should adopt is that knowledge has one relationship, which is the pre-eternal actual relationship, which we attribute to the majority of theologians. And our Lord God knows things in general and in detail, and He is knowing of universals and particulars, and He knows what has no end, such as His perfections and the breaths of the people of Paradise; He knows them in detail and knows that they are infinite. As for the claim that detailed knowledge depends on the thing being finite, and that what is infinite cannot be known in detail, this is relative to our intellects. His knowledge of Himself is included in what His knowledge relates to, for He, exalted, knows that He has knowledge. The philosophers held that He does not know particulars, and this is one of three issues for which they were deemed disbelievers: the second is their claim of the eternity of the world, and the third is their denial of the resurrection of bodies and their claim that what is resurrected is the souls. Some of them versified these issues in his saying: 'By three things the hostile philosophers disbelieved when they denied them: knowledge of a particular, origination of worlds, resurrection of bodies that were dead.' End of al-Mahi.
Source pages p. 249

Commentary
This passage presents proofs that God knows all things, including particulars, and refutes the philosophers who deny His knowledge of particulars. It begins by stating that every possible object of knowledge is known to God, then gives two arguments: first, God acts with flawless precision, and whoever does so must be knowing; second, God acts with purpose and choice, which requires knowledge of the intended object. It then clarifies the nature of divine knowledge: it is one eternal attribute encompassing all things in general and in detail, including infinite things like God's perfections and the infinite breaths of Paradise. The claim that detailed knowledge requires finitude is rejected as a limitation of human intellect. The passage then addresses the philosophers' denial of God's knowledge of particulars, listing it as the first of three issues for which they were deemed disbelievers, the others being the eternity of the world and denial of bodily resurrection. A poetic summary of these issues is provided.

It Is Impermissible to Say God's Knowledge Is Acquired
Analysis of the phrase 'yqal' and rational proof for the impossibility of acquired knowledge in God.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the correct interpretation of the phrase 'it is not said' regarding God's knowledge, and the rational impossibility of acquired knowledge for Allah.

Core Idea
Acquired knowledge implies prior ignorance, which is impossible for God.

The author addresses a delicate point: can we say that God's knowledge is 'acquired'? The common sense might be to reject it outright, but the reasoning goes deeper. The word 'yqal' (it is said) can be understood in two ways, and the correct understanding reveals both a legal and a rational prohibition. This lesson unpacks the argument and the proper interpretation.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Carefully read the Arabic passage and its literal translation. Notice the two possible interpretations of the word 'yqal' that the text discusses.
The passage is dense, so take your time.
2
Identify the two meanings of 'yqal'
The passage explains that 'yqal' can mean 'it is said' (factual description) or 'it is believed'. See how each leads to a different understanding of the prohibition.
The preferred interpretation is 'belief', making the prohibition both legal and rational.
3
Understand the rational argument
Why is acquired knowledge impossible for God? Because it requires prior ignorance, and God is eternally knowing. Grasp this logical connection.
This is the core of the rational proof.

⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الاستدلال استحالة كون علم الله مكتسبا (وَلَا يُقَالُ) أي: ولا يجوز شرعاً أن يطلق على علمه تعالى بالمعنى. كلمة «يقال» تطلق على المعنى الذي يعبر عنه بنحو (يصدق) و(يطلق) وربما أريد منها (يعتقد)، فإذا فسرت هذه الكلمة بالمعنى الأول كما فعل الشارح صار المعنى: لا يجوز شرعاً ولا عقلا أن يطلق ويحمل لفظ (مكتسب) على علم الله تعالى فيقال: علمه سبحانه مكتسب، وهذا ربما يوهم أن المنهي عنه هو هذا القول مع أن المعنى صحيح؛ وأن علة النهي هي عدم ورود إذن من الشارع بالإطلاق، ومن أجل هذا كان الأحسن أن نفسر كلمة «يقال» في عبارة المصنف بالاعتقاد، وعلى هذا يكون المعنى: ولا يجوز لأحد أن يعتقد أن علمه مكتسب؛ وذلك لاستحالة ذلك بالنظر إلى الله تعالى من قبل أن المكتسب والكسبي في عرف أهل العلم: هو العلم الذي يحصل بعد النظر والاستدلال، فإن أقام أحد دليلا على حدوث العالم؛ كان العلم الحاصل له بعد هذا الدليل علما مكتسبا أو كسبيا. وإنما كان العلم المكتسب مستحيلا في حقه تعالى، لكونه يقتضي سبق الجهل، إذ ما يحصل النظر والاستدلال لا يحصل العلم.
Reasoning: The impossibility of God's knowledge being acquired. 'And it is not said' means: it is not permissible legally to apply to His knowledge in the sense... The word 'yqal' applies to the meaning expressed by (it holds true) and (it is applied), and perhaps it is intended to mean (it is believed). If you interpret this word in the first sense as the commentator did, the meaning becomes: it is not permissible legally or rationally to apply and predicate the term 'acquired' to God's knowledge, saying 'His knowledge is acquired'. This might give the impression that the prohibited thing is this statement, even though the meaning is correct, and that the cause of the prohibition is the lack of permission from the Lawgiver for the application. For this reason, it is better to interpret the word 'yqal' in the author's phrase as belief. On this interpretation, the meaning is: it is not permissible for anyone to believe that His knowledge is acquired, because of its impossibility with respect to God, due to the fact that 'acquired' and 'acquired-by-effort' in the terminology of scholars is knowledge that comes after reasoning and deduction. If someone presents a proof for the temporal origination of the world, the knowledge he obtains after this proof is acquired or earned knowledge. Acquired knowledge is impossible in God's case because it requires the precedence of ignorance, since knowledge does not occur through reasoning and deduction.
Source pages p. 250

Commentary
This passage rules on the statement that God's knowledge is acquired. It begins by stating that it is legally impermissible to apply the term 'acquired' to God's knowledge. The main discussion then centers on the correct interpretation of the word 'yqal' ('it is said'). The passage explains that this word can be understood in two ways: as a factual description (it holds true) or as belief. If taken as a factual description, the prohibition is only legal (due to lack of scriptural permission) but the meaning could be correct. However, the better interpretation is that 'yqal' here means 'it is believed', so the prohibition is both rational and legal: it is impermissible to believe that God's knowledge is acquired. The reason is that acquired knowledge in scholarly terminology is knowledge that results from reasoning and deduction, which implies prior ignorance. Since God is necessarily knowing from eternity, such knowledge is impossible for Him.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that the prohibition against saying 'God's knowledge is acquired' is merely a matter of proper speech (legal) and that the concept is rationally acceptable.
Correction: The passage clarifies that the prohibition is not just legal but also rational: acquired knowledge for God is impossible because it entails prior ignorance.

Key Takeaway
God's knowledge cannot be acquired because it would imply prior ignorance, which is impossible for Him.

?
Acquired knowledge implies that God would need to 'figure out' or 'discover' something about you. Is there any area of your life where you act as if God is surprised by your choices? How will you remind yourself daily that His knowledge is immediate and complete, so that you live with more seriousness and sincerity?
