The Question and First Four Interpretive Answers
Sanusi presents the objection and four answers to verses implying God acquires knowledge.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how classical scholars interpret Quranic verses that appear to attribute knowledge acquisition to Allah, reconciling them with divine omniscience through four distinct interpretive approaches.

Core Idea
Quranic verses that seem to imply God learns are reinterpreted to preserve His eternal knowledge.

This passage presents a theological objection based on several Quranic verses whose apparent meaning suggests that Allah acquires knowledge through events. The scholars respond with four interpretive answers, each showing how the verses can be understood without compromising Allah's omniscience. As you read, notice the different strategies they employ—ranging from attributive language to metaphor—to defend the divine attribute of eternal knowledge.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection and the cited verses
Identify the objection: The apparent meaning of verses like 'that We might know' suggests God gains knowledge. Observe the list of Quranic proofs.
2
Analyze the first answer: The 'king analogy'
Notice how the first answer reinterprets the action as belonging to others (Messenger, believers) but attributed to Allah as a figure of authority.
Compare with the king saying 'we conquered' when his army did.
3
Examine the second answer: The actualization of eternal knowledge
Understand that Allah's knowledge pre-exists, but events externalize what is known, allowing knowledge to attach in time.
This involves a temporal aspect of knowledge attachment.
4
Explore the third answer: Knowledge as metaphor for distinction
See how 'knowledge' here means making distinctions among people based on their hidden sincerity or hypocrisy.
This serves a practical purpose for believers.
5
Consider the fourth answer: Knowledge as metaphor for seeing
Arabic usage allows 'knowing' to mean 'seeing', as both are interchangeable.
Quranic examples illustrate this linguistic phenomenon.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قسم الإلهيات
فإن قلت: فقد وردت في القرآن آيات كثيرة يوهم ظاهرها اكتساب الله تعالى العلم، نحو قوله سبحانه: ﴿ثُمَّ بَعَثْنَهُمْ لِنَعْلَمَ أَيُّ الْحِزْبَيْنِ أَحْصَى لِمَا لَبِثُوا أَمَدًا﴾ [الكهف: ١٢]، ألا ترى أن ظاهر الكلام يقتضي أنه تعالى إنَّما بعثهم ليحصل له هذا العلم؟ ويرجع الأمر إلى أنه سبحانه يستفيد ببعثهم علما لم يكن حاصلا له. ونظائر هذه الآية كثيرةٌ في القرآن منها قوله في سورة البقرة: ﴿إِلَّا لِنَعْلَمَ مَن يَتَّبِعُ الرَّسُولَ مِمَّن يَنقَلِبُ عَلَى عَقِبَيْهِ﴾ [البقرة: ١٤٣]، وقوله في سورة آل عمران: ﴿أَمْ حَسِبْتُمْ أَن تَدْخُلُوا الْجَنَّةَ وَلَمَّا يَعْلَمِ اللَّهُ الَّذِينَ جَهَدُوا مِنكُمْ وَيَعْلَمَ الصَّابِرِينَ﴾ [آل عمران: ١٤٢]، وقوله جلّت كلمته: ﴿إِنَّا جَعَلْنَا مَا عَلَى الْأَرْضِ زِينَةً لَهَا لِنَبْلُوَهُمْ﴾ [الكهف: ٧]، وقوله: ﴿وَلَنَبْلُوَنَّكُم حَتَّى نَعْلَمَ الْمُجَهِدِينَ مِنكُمْ وَالصَّابِرِينَ﴾ [محمد: ٣١] وقوله: ﴿الْفَنَ خَفَّفَ اللهُ عَنكُمْ وَعَلِمَ أَنَّ فِيكُمْ ضَعْفَا﴾ [الأنفال: ٦٦]، وقوله: ﴿وَمَا كَانَ لَهُ عَلَيْهِم مِّن سُلْطانِ إِلَّا لِنَعْلَمَ مَن يُؤْمِنُ بِالآخِرَة﴾ [سبأ: ٢١]. فالجواب أن نقول لك: إن ظاهر هذه الآيات ونحوها غير مرادٍ وقد أجاب العلماء عنها بعدة أجوبة غير الذي ذكره الشارح:
أحدها: أن مراد الله تعالى أن يَعلَم الرَّسول ومَن معه مِن المؤمنين، فأسند الفعل إلى نفسه وهو يريد حزبه وأولياءه، وذلك كما يقول الملك فعلنا كذا وفتحنا البلد الفلاني، وليس هو الفاعل، ولا الفاتح، وإنما الفاعل عُمَّاله والفاتح جيشه، ونحن نقول الآن: فتح عمر بن الخطاب له سواد العراق.
والثَّاني: أن معنى الكلام ليحصل ما هو معلوم لنا، فيصير موجودا في الخارج، فيتعلّق علمنا بوجوده كما تعلّق به أزلا على أنَّه سيوجد، فإن معلوم الله لا يمكن أن يتعلق علمه بوجوده قبل أن يوجد، وقد تقدم ذكر ذلك في بيان تعلق العلم.
والثالث: أن العلم ههنا مجاز، والمراد إلا لتَميُّز هؤلاء بانكشاف ما في قلوبهم من إخلاص أو نفاق، حتى يتسنى للمؤمنين أن يوالوا منهم من يستحق الموالاة، ويعادوا منهم من يستحق المعاداة.
والرابع: أنّ العلم ههنا مجاز عن الرُّؤية، والعرب تضع العلم مكان الرؤية، وتضع الرؤية مكان العلم، وقد ورد ذلك في القرآن الكريم في نحو قوله تعالى: ﴿أَلَمْ تَرَكَيْفَ فَعَلَ رَبُّكَ﴾ [الفجر: ٦]، وعلم، وشهد، ورأى ألفاظ متعاقبة يقع بعضها موقع بعض.
Section of Divine Matters. If you say: There have come in the Quran many verses whose apparent meaning suggests that Allah acquires knowledge, such as His saying: 'Then We raised them up that We might know which of the two parties was best at counting the length of their stay' [18:12]. Do you not see that the apparent sense of the statement requires that He raised them up so that this knowledge might be obtained for Him? And the matter reduces to the idea that He, by raising them, derives knowledge that was not previously His. And similar verses are many in the Quran, including His saying in Surat al-Baqara: 'except that We might know who follows the Messenger from him who turns back on his heels' [2:143], and His saying in Surat Al Imran: 'Or did you think you would enter Paradise while Allah has not yet known those who strive among you and known the patient' [3:142], and His saying: 'Indeed We have made that which is on the earth an adornment for it that We may test them' [18:7], and His saying: 'And We will surely test you until We know the strivers among you and the patient' [47:31], and His saying: 'Now Allah has lightened your burden and knows that there is weakness among you' [8:66], and His saying: 'And he had no authority over them except that We might know who believes in the Hereafter' [34:21]. So the answer is that we say to you: The apparent meaning of these verses and the like is not intended, and the scholars have answered them with several answers other than the one the commentator mentioned: First: That Allah intended that the Messenger and the believers with him should know, so He attributed the action to Himself while meaning His party and friends. This is like when a king says 'We did such-and-such and conquered such-and-such city,' while he is not the actual doer or conqueror; rather, the doer is his officials and the conqueror is his army. And we now say: 'Umar ibn al-Khattab conquered the Sawad of Iraq for him. Second: That the meaning of the statement is: so that what is known to us might occur, and become existent externally, so that our knowledge becomes attached to its existence as it was attached to it in eternity when it was to exist. For Allah's knowledge cannot attach to its existence before it exists, as has been previously mentioned in the explanation of the attachment of knowledge. Third: That 'knowledge' here is metaphorical, and the intended meaning is only to distinguish these people by revealing what is in their hearts of sincerity or hypocrisy, so that the believers may be able to ally with those who deserve alliance and oppose those who deserve opposition. Fourth: That 'knowledge' here is metaphorical for 'seeing,' and the Arabs place 'knowledge' in the position of 'seeing' and place 'seeing' in the position of 'knowledge.' This has occurred in the Quran, as in His saying: 'Did you not see how your Lord dealt with...' [89:6]. And 'knowledge,' 'witnessing,' and 'seeing' are interchangeable words, one occurring in place of another.
Source pages p. 251

Commentary
This passage presents an objection and four interpretive answers. The objector cites multiple Quranic verses whose apparent wording attributes acquisition of knowledge to Allah, such as 'that We might know...' The objection claims these verses imply that Allah gains new knowledge, which would contradict His omniscience. The response begins by stating that the apparent meaning is not intended, and scholars have offered several answers. The first answer uses the analogy of a king attributing an action to himself when his agents perform it; similarly, Allah attributes 'knowing' to Himself but means His Messenger and believers. The second answer explains that the verb 'know' refers to the actualization of what is eternally known; Allah's knowledge of an event before its occurrence is already there, but the event's external existence allows His knowledge to attach to it in a temporal sense. The third answer treats 'knowledge' as a metaphor for distinguishing sincere from hypocritical individuals, facilitating believers' alliances and oppositions. The fourth answer considers 'knowledge' a metaphor for 'seeing,' citing Arabic linguistic usage where 'ilm, shahada, and ru'ya are interchangeable, as in Quran 89:6. Each answer preserves Allah's eternal knowledge while interpreting the verses in a way consistent with His transcendence.

العلم
اكتساب العلم
ظاهر الآيات
مجاز
الرؤية
الشهادة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Quranic verses like 'that We might know' mean Allah acquires new knowledge after events.
Correction: Scholars reinterpret these verses to show they do not imply acquisition; rather, knowledge is eternal and these phrases are attributive or metaphorical.

Checkpoint
What is the main purpose of the four answers given in response to the objection?
A) To deny the authenticity of the verses

D) To claim that the verses are abrogated

Key Takeaway
The four answers demonstrate that apparent Quranic attributions of knowledge acquisition to Allah are reinterpreted through figurative language and contextual attribution to uphold His omniscience.

God's Knowledge Cannot Be Acquired
Sanusi gives a rational argument against God's knowledge being acquired.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the rational argument used by the Ash'aris to prove that God's knowledge is eternal and not acquired, and how they interpret apparent scriptural references to acquisition.

Core Idea
Acquired knowledge is temporal, but God's knowledge is eternal—so they cannot coexist.

This lesson presents one of the central rational proofs in Ash'ari theology: that God's knowledge cannot be acquired. The argument is built on the logical incompatibility between the nature of acquired knowledge (which is temporal) and the nature of God (who is eternal). By working through this proof, you will see how the Ash'aris defend God's timeless knowledge while engaging with scriptural verses that might suggest otherwise.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then the literal translation. Note the key terms like 'kasbi' (acquired) and 'hadith' (temporal).
The passage is dense, so take your time.
2
Follow the logical steps of the proof
Identify the premise about acquired knowledge, the deduction that it must be temporal, the implications for God's essence (subsistence of accidents and prior ignorance), and the conclusion of impossibility.
Try to reconstruct the syllogism in your own words.
3
Understand the scriptural interpretation
The passage mentions Quran 18:12 and explains how the Ash'aris interpret the lam as indicating consequence, not divine purpose. Reflect on why this interpretation is necessary.
This shows how rational proofs guide scriptural exegesis.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
استحالة كون علم الله مكتسبا
السَّابق إِنَّه مُكْتَسَبْ لأنَّ الكَسبيَّ لا يكونُ إلَّا حادثا، وعلمه تعالى قديم لا يتجدد، والكسبي - عرفًا - هو: العلم الحاصل عن النظر والاستدلال، أو ما تعلّقت به القدرة الحادثة، وعليهما فلا بدَّ مِن تجدُّدِه وحدوثه؛ فيستلزم قیامه به تعالى قيام الحوادث بذاته، وسَبْقَ جهله تعالى بما اكتسب علمه، وهو محالٌ، فما أوهم الاكتساب كقوله تعالى: ﴿ثُمَّ بَعَثْنَهُمْ لِنَعْلَمَ﴾ [الكهف: ١٢] مؤوَّلٌ عند الأشاعرة على جعل لامه للعاقبة والفائدة، والمعنى: فعلنا.
The impossibility of God's knowledge being acquired. The aforementioned [knowledge] is acquired, because the acquired can only be originated, and His knowledge is eternal and does not renew. The acquired, conventionally, is knowledge resulting from reflection and inference, or that to which an originated power attaches. On both definitions, it must be renewed and originated; so its subsisting in Him necessitates the subsistence of originated events in His essence, and the precedence of His ignorance of what He acquired knowledge of—and that is impossible. So what suggests acquisition, like His saying: 'Then We raised them up that We might know' [18:12], is interpreted by the Ash'aris to make its lam for consequence and benefit, and the meaning: We did.
Source pages p. 252

Commentary
This passage is a rational proof (burhan) demonstrating the logical impossibility of God's knowledge being acquired. The argument proceeds as follows: Acquired knowledge (kasbi) is by definition something that occurs only as a temporal event (hadith). It is either the result of reasoning (nazar and istidlal) or dependent on an originated power (qudra haditha). In both cases, it must be renewed and originated. If such knowledge were to subsist in God, it would entail the subsistence of temporal events in His essence (qiyam al-hawadith bi-dhatihi), which is impossible for God. Moreover, it would imply that God was previously ignorant of what He later acquired knowledge of—another impossibility. Therefore, any apparent suggestion of acquisition in the Quran must be interpreted figuratively. The Ash'aris interpret the lam in verses like 'li-na'lam' as indicating consequence or benefit, not purpose from God. The text then gives a brief example: 'the meaning: We did...' (perhaps completing an analogy). This proof establishes the core Ash'ari position that God's knowledge is eternal and unchanging.

Key Terms
الاستحالة
الكسبي
الحادث
القديم
قيام الحوادث بالذات
أشاعرة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that 'acquisition' in kalam refers only to human acts, so it is not relevant to God.
Correction: The argument shows that even if applied to God, the concept of acquisition is incompatible with His eternity, regardless of whether it is used for humans.

Checkpoint
Why does the argument claim that acquired knowledge cannot belong to God?
A) Because it would mean God changes His mind
B) Because acquired knowledge is temporal and God is eternal
C) Because God does not use reasoning
D) Because the Quran explicitly denies it

Key Takeaway
God's knowledge cannot be acquired because acquired knowledge is necessarily temporal, while God's knowledge is eternal and unchanging.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you sometimes live as if your knowledge of God is incomplete and needs to be 'acquired' through your own efforts, forgetting that His knowledge of you is eternal and perfect? How would it change your trust in Him if you truly internalized that He has never been ignorant of anything?
?
When you face trials or unanswered questions, do you act as if God's knowledge has 'renewed' or changed regarding your situation? Are you willing to submit to the eternal, unchanging knowledge of God even when you don't understand His decree?
?
What would it mean for your daily decisions if you believed that God's knowledge is so complete that no acquisition is possible? Would you still fear the unknown, or would you rest in His eternal awareness?

Fifth and Sixth Answers and Conclusion
Sanusi gives the fifth and sixth answers and concludes.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the fifth and sixth interpretive answers that resolve apparent anthropomorphic or acquisition-of-knowledge verses, and the author's concluding remark.

Core Idea
God's 'knowing' in tests is for our benefit, not His.

In the previous lesson, Sanusi gave four answers to verses that seem to attribute the act of 'learning' to Allah. Here he completes the list with two more answers, one based on al-Farra's explanation that the knowledge pertains to the addressees, and another that treats such verses as parables. He then concludes the section, noting that sufficient answers have been presented.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice the analogy used by al-Farra and the reference to Quran 34:24.
2
Understand the fifth answer
Focus on al-Farra's explanation: the 'knowledge' in 'li-na'lam' actually refers to the addressees, not Allah. Note the analogy of the ignorant and the scholar.
The purpose is rhetorical gentleness, not literal acquisition.
3
Understand the sixth and conclusion
The sixth answer interprets the verse as a parable (tamthil). Then note the author's concluding remark that other answers exist but this sample suffices.
The word 'maqna'u' means sufficient.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والخامس: ـ وهو ما ذكره الفراء - أنَّ حدوث العِلم راجع إلى المخاطبين، ومثاله: أن جاهلا وعالما اجتمعا، فقال الجاهل: الحطب يحرق النَّار، وقال: العالم: النار تحرق الحطب، وآية ذلك أن نجمع بينهما لنعلم أيُّهما يحرق صاحبَه، مع أنَّ العالم يدرك قبل الجمع بينهما أنَّ النار هي التي تحرق، فمراده من الكلام الأوّل، لتعلم أيها المخاطب علم المشاهدة أيهما يحرقُ الآخر، فكذلك قوله تعالى: «لنعلم» معناه لتعلموا أنتم، والغرض من مثل هذا الكلام الاستمالة بالرفق في الخطاب، كما في نحو قوله سبحانه: ﴿وَإِنَّا أَوْ إِيَّاكُمْ لَعَلَى هُدًى أَوْ فِي ضَلَالٍ مُّبِينٍ﴾ [سبأ: ٢٤]، فأضاف سبحانه الكلام الموهم للشك إلى نفسه ترقيقا للخطاب ورفقا بالمخاطب.
والسادس: أنَّ الكلام على سبيل التمثيل؛ ومعناه: فعَلْنا ذلك فعلَ مَن يريد أن يعلم، وفي الآيات أجوبة أخرى وهذا نموذج منها وفيه كفاية ومَقَنْعُ.
And the fifth: which is what al-Farra mentioned—that the occurrence of knowledge refers back to the addressees. Its example is: An ignorant person and a scholar met. The ignorant said: 'Firewood burns the fire,' and the scholar said: 'The fire burns the firewood.' The sign of that is that we bring them together to know which of them burns its companion, even though the scholar knows before bringing them together that the fire is what burns. So his intention from the first statement is: that you, the addressee, may know through observational knowledge which one burns the other. Similarly, His saying, the Exalted, 'li-na'lam' means: that you yourselves may know. The purpose of such speech is to attract by gentleness in address, as in His saying: 'Indeed, we or you are either upon guidance or in manifest error' [34:24]. So He added the speech that suggests doubt to Himself to soften the address and be gentle with the addressee. And the sixth: that the speech is in the manner of a parable; its meaning is: We did that as one who wants to know does. And there are other answers to the verses, and this is a sample of them, and in it is sufficiency and satisfaction.
Source pages p. 252

Commentary
This passage presents the fifth and sixth interpretive answers, then concludes. The fifth answer, attributed to al-Farra, holds that the occurrence of knowledge ('ilm) actually pertains to the addressees (the believers), not to God. Al-Farra illustrates with an analogy: an ignorant person and a scholar talk about fire and wood; when they test it, the observer 'knows' which burns, even though the scholar already knew. So the verse's 'li-na'lam' means 'so that you (people) may know' — the attribution to God is a rhetorical device to show gentleness, similar to Quran 34:24 where God attributes a statement of doubt to Himself to soften the tone. The sixth answer interprets the verse as a parable (tamthil): God's action is compared to someone who wants to know, without actual intent of ignorance. Finally, the author notes that there are other answers, but this sample suffices. Thus, all six answers protect God from the attribute of acquired knowledge.

Key Terms
الفراء
المخاطب
التمثيل
الاستمالة
الرفق في الخطاب

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think God literally acquires knowledge from the test.
Correction: The answers show the test is for us or is a parable; God does not acquire knowledge.

Checkpoint
According to al-Farra's answer, to whom does the 'knowledge' in 'li-na'lam' actually refer?
A) To Allah only
B) To the angels
C) To the addressees (the people)
D) To the Prophet Muhammad

Key Takeaway
The fifth and sixth answers show that apparent 'acquired knowledge' verses are rhetorical devices for our benefit or parables, preserving God's omniscience.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you read Quranic verses that seem to attribute human limitations to God, do you quickly dismiss them or pause to understand the divine wisdom behind such rhetoric? How can you train yourself to see these as acts of gentleness from God toward you?
?
The passage shows that God uses a style of speech that suggests doubt to be gentle with us. Have you ever considered that God's way of communicating takes your weakness into account? How does this change your attitude when you encounter difficult verses?
?
If God, the All-Knowing, chooses to speak to you in a way that is gentle and patient, what does that imply about how you should speak to others—especially those who are ignorant? Will you adopt a similar gentleness when explaining truth?

Proof That God's Knowledge Cannot Be Acquired
Sanusi presents a syllogistic proof against acquired divine knowledge.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the logical proof that God's knowledge cannot be acquired because acquired knowledge is temporal while God's knowledge is eternal.

Core Idea
Acquired knowledge is temporal; God's knowledge is eternal.

Sanusi begins by refuting the notion that God's knowledge can be acquired (kasb). He presents a straightforward syllogism: acquired knowledge is necessarily temporal, but God's knowledge is eternal and unchanging, so acquisition is impossible. This lesson unpacks that argument and its implications for divine transcendence.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage and its translation
Read the passage carefully to grasp Sanusi's concise argument. Notice the key terms: 'kasbī' (acquired), 'ḥādith' (temporal), and 'qadīm' (eternal).
The argument is a simple syllogism—identify the premises.
2
Identify the premises of the proof
Locate the two definitions of 'acquired' (from reflection or temporal power). Note that both definitions require temporality. Then see how Sanusi applies this to God's knowledge.
3
Understand the conclusion and its implications
If acquired knowledge were to subsist in God, it would imply temporal events in His essence and prior ignorance—both impossible. This safeguards God's perfection.
Reflect on why this matters for Tawḥīd.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
استحالة كون علم الله مكتسبا السَّابق إِنَّه مُكْتَسَبْ) لأنَّ الكَسبيَّ لا يكونُ إلَّا حادثا ، وعلمه تعالى قديم لا يتجدد ، والكسبي - عرفًا - هو : العلم الحاصل عن النظر والاستدلال، أو ما تعلّقت به القدرة الحادثة ، وعليهما فلا بدَّ مِن تجدُّدِه وحدوثه ؛ فيستلزم قیامه به تعالى قيام الحوادث بذاته ، وسَبْقَ جهله تعالى بما اكتسب علمه ، وهو محالٌ ، فما أوهم الاكتساب كقوله تعالى: ﴿ثُمَّ بَعَثْنَهُمْ لِنَعْلَمَ ﴾ [الكهف: ١٢] مؤوَّلٌ عند الأشاعرة على جعل لامه للعاقبة والفائدة ، والمعنى: فعلنا
والخامس: ـ وهو ما ذكره الفراء - أنَّ حدوث العِلم راجع إلى المخاطبين، ومثاله: أن جاهلا وعالما اجتمعا ، فقال الجاهل : الحطب يحرق النَّار ، وقال :العالم: النار تحرق الحطب، وآية ذلك أن نجمع بينهما لنعلم أيُّهما يحرق ،صاحبَه ، مع أنَّ العالم يدرك قبل الجمع بينهما أنَّ النار هي التي تحرق، فمراده من الكلام الأوّل ، لتعلم أيها المخاطب علم المشاهدة أيهما يحرقُ الآخر ، فكذلك قوله تعالى: «النعلم معناه لتعلموا أنتم ، والغرض من مثل هذا الكلام الاستمالة بالرفق في الخطاب، كما في نحو قوله سبحانه: ﴿وَإِنَّا أَوْ إِيَّاكُمْ لَعَلَى هُدًى أَوْ فِي ضَلَالٍ مُّبِينٍ ﴾ [سبأ: ٢٤]، فأضاف سبحانه الكلام الموهم للشك إلى نفسه ترقيقا للخطاب ورفقا بالمخاطب.
والسادس: أنَّ الكلام على سبيل التمثيل ؛ ومعناه : فعَلْنا ذلك فعلَ مَن يريد أن يعلم، وفي الآيات أجوبة أخرى وهذا نموذج منها وفيه كفاية ومَقَنْعُ . ذلك فترتب عليه فوائد ومصالح غيرُ باعثة على الفعل، لكنها مترتبة عليه ترتب الاستظلال مثلاً على الشَّجر المَغْرَوس ، من غير أن يكون حاملا على غَرْسِه ، وإِنَّما الحامل عليه الانتفاع بثمرته .
The impossibility of God's knowledge being acquired. The preceding [statement] that it is acquired [is false] because the acquired cannot be except temporal, and His knowledge, exalted, is eternal, not renewed. The acquired, conventionally, is knowledge obtained from reflection and inference, or that to which temporal power is attached. According to both [definitions], it must be renewed and temporal; thus its subsistence in Him, exalted, entails the subsistence of temporal events in His essence, and the precedence of ignorance in Him, exalted, regarding what He acquired knowledge of, and that is impossible. So what gives the impression of acquisition, like His saying, exalted: 'Then We raised them up that We might know' [al-Kahf:12], is interpreted by the Ash'aris as making its lām for consequence and benefit, and the meaning: We did... And the fifth – which al-Farrā' mentioned – that the occurrence of knowledge refers to the addressees. Its example: an ignorant and a knowledgeable person met; the ignorant said: 'Firewood burns fire,' and the knowledgeable said: 'Fire burns firewood.' The proof of that is that we gather them together to know which one burns its companion, even though the knowledgeable person knows before gathering them that fire is what burns. So his intention from the first statement is that you, the addressee, may know by observational knowledge which one burns the other. Similarly, His saying, exalted: 'that We might know' means 'that you might know.' The purpose of such speech is to draw sympathy with gentleness in address, as in His saying, exalted: 'Indeed, we or you are either upon guidance or in manifest error' [Saba':24]. So He, exalted, attributed the speech that gives the impression of doubt to Himself, softening the address and being gentle with the addressee. And the sixth: that the speech is by way of similitude; its meaning: We did that as one who wants to know does. And in the verses there are other answers, and this is a sample of them, and in it is sufficiency and satisfaction. That [action] then resulted in benefits and interests not motivating the act, but rather consequent upon it, like shadowing, for example, upon a planted tree, without it being a motive for its planting; rather, the motive for it is benefiting from its fruit.
Source pages p. 252

Commentary
This passage is a proof and an objection-and-answer section. The author first states the impossibility of God's knowledge being acquired (kasb). The argument: acquired knowledge is necessarily temporal (hadith), but God's knowledge is eternal (qadim) and does not undergo renewal. The acquired, by definition, is knowledge obtained through reflection and inference, or knowledge to which a temporal power attaches; in both cases, it must be temporal. If it were to subsist in God, it would imply temporal events in His essence and that He was ignorant before acquiring it, which is impossible. The author then addresses a potential objection: the Qur'anic verse 'Then We raised them up that We might know' (al-Kahf 12) seems to attribute acquisition to God. The author states it is interpreted (mu'awwal) by the Ash'aris. He provides three interpretations: First, the lām (for) is for consequence and benefit, meaning the action resulted in benefits not as a motive (like shade from a tree). This interpretation emphasizes that the 'knowing' is not an acquisition but a result. Second (the fifth interpretation attributed to al-Farrā'): the attribution of knowledge is to the addressees, not to God. The example illustrates that the apparent meaning of 'knowing' is for the audience to witness. Third (the sixth): the speech is by way of similitude (tamthil), meaning God did what one who wants to know does. The passage concludes that other answers exist, but these suffice. The author then returns to the first interpretation's explanation: the action results in benefits that are not the motive but consequent, like shade from a tree.

Key Terms
كسبي
حادث
قديم

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think that God's knowledge can be acquired through learning or revelation.
Correction: The proof shows acquired knowledge is temporal, but God's knowledge is eternal and unchanging, so it cannot be acquired.

Checkpoint
Why is it impossible for God's knowledge to be acquired (kasb)?
A) Because acquired knowledge is temporal, but God's knowledge is eternal.
B) Because God does not need knowledge.
C) Because knowledge is a contingent accident.
D) Because the Qur'an forbids it.

Key Takeaway
God's knowledge is eternal and unchanging; any notion of acquisition implies temporality and is therefore impossible.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you catch yourself ever thinking that God 'finds out' about your deeds after you do them, as if His knowledge were acquired? How does this passage force you to reject that thought, and what should you do when it arises?
?
If God's knowledge is eternal and not acquired, then He has always known every sin you would commit. Does that make you feel hopeless, or does it deepen your reliance on His mercy? Be honest: which feeling is closer to your heart right now?
?
The proof says that acquired knowledge would imply prior ignorance in God. Have you ever prayed as if you were informing God of something new? How can you align your prayer with the truth that He already knows everything eternally?

Interpreting the Verse ‘That We Might Know’
Sanusi provides three Ash'ari interpretations of the verse.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Ash'ari scholars interpret the Qur'anic verse 'that We might know' to avoid attributing temporal knowledge to Allah.

Core Idea
The verse is interpreted metaphorically to preserve divine transcendence.

Having established that God's knowledge cannot be acquired, Sanusi addresses a potential objection: the Qur'anic verse that seems to imply Allah acquires knowledge. He presents several Ash'ari interpretations, including the lām of consequence, attribution to addressees, and similitude. This lesson explores these hermeneutical strategies and their rationale.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection and the verse
Read the Arabic passage starting from 'فما أوهم الاكتساب'. Notice the verse from Surah al-Kahf: 12. Understand why it appears to contradict the proof.
The verse literally says 'that We might know', implying acquisition.
2
Study the first interpretation (lām al-ʿāqiba)
Learn how the lām is interpreted as for consequence and benefit, not purpose. The action results in knowledge as a side effect, not an acquisition.
3
Study the fifth and sixth interpretations
The fifth interpretation attributes the 'knowing' to the addressees (you may know). The sixth says it is a similitude (tamthīl) meaning God acts as one who wants to know does.
Notice the example of the tree at the end illustrates the first interpretation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

والسادس: أنَّ الكلام على سبيل التمثيل ؛ ومعناه : فعَلْنا ذلك فعلَ مَن يريد أن يعلم، وفي الآيات أجوبة أخرى وهذا نموذج منها وفيه كفاية ومَقَنْعُ . ذلك فترتب عليه فوائد ومصالح غيرُ باعثة على الفعل، لكنها مترتبة عليه ترتب الاستظلال مثلاً على الشَّجر المَغْرَوس ، من غير أن يكون حاملا على غَرْسِه ، وإِنَّما الحامل عليه الانتفاع بثمرته .
The impossibility of God's knowledge being acquired. The preceding [statement] that it is acquired [is false] because the acquired cannot be except temporal, and His knowledge, exalted, is eternal, not renewed. The acquired, conventionally, is knowledge obtained from reflection and inference, or that to which temporal power is attached. According to both [definitions], it must be renewed and temporal; thus its subsistence in Him, exalted, entails the subsistence of temporal events in His essence, and the precedence of ignorance in Him, exalted, regarding what He acquired knowledge of, and that is impossible. So what gives the impression of acquisition, like His saying, exalted: 'Then We raised them up that We might know' [al-Kahf:12], is interpreted by the Ash'aris as making its lām for consequence and benefit, and the meaning: We did... And the fifth – which al-Farrā' mentioned – that the occurrence of knowledge refers to the addressees. Its example: an ignorant and a knowledgeable person met; the ignorant said: 'Firewood burns fire,' and the knowledgeable said: 'Fire burns firewood.' The proof of that is that we gather them together to know which one burns its companion, even though the knowledgeable person knows before gathering them that fire is what burns. So his intention from the first statement is that you, the addressee, may know by observational knowledge which one burns the other. Similarly, His saying, exalted: 'that We might know' means 'that you might know.' The purpose of such speech is to draw sympathy with gentleness in address, as in His saying, exalted: 'Indeed, we or you are either upon guidance or in manifest error' [Saba':24]. So He, exalted, attributed the speech that gives the impression of doubt to Himself, softening the address and being gentle with the addressee. And the sixth: that the speech is by way of similitude; its meaning: We did that as one who wants to know does. And in the verses there are other answers, and this is a sample of them, and in it is sufficiency and satisfaction. That [action] then resulted in benefits and interests not motivating the act, but rather consequent upon it, like shadowing, for example, upon a planted tree, without it being a motive for its planting; rather, the motive for it is benefiting from its fruit.
Source pages p. 252

Commentary
This passage is a proof and an objection-and-answer section. The author first states the impossibility of God's knowledge being acquired (kasb). The argument: acquired knowledge is necessarily temporal (hadith), but God's knowledge is eternal (qadim) and does not undergo renewal. The acquired, by definition, is knowledge obtained through reflection and inference, or knowledge to which a temporal power attaches; in both cases, it must be temporal. If it were to subsist in God, it would imply temporal events in His essence and that He was ignorant before acquiring it, which is impossible. The author then addresses a potential objection: the Qur'anic verse 'Then We raised them up that We might know' (al-Kahf 12) seems to attribute acquisition to God. The author states it is interpreted (mu'awwal) by the Ash'aris. He provides three interpretations: First, the lām (for) is for consequence and benefit, meaning the action resulted in benefits not as a motive (like shade from a tree). This interpretation emphasizes that the 'knowing' is not an acquisition but a result. Second (the fifth interpretation attributed to al-Farrā'): the attribution of knowledge is to the addressees, not to God. The example illustrates that the apparent meaning of 'knowing' is for the audience to witness. Third (the sixth): the speech is by way of similitude (tamthil), meaning God did what one who wants to know does. The passage concludes that other answers exist, but these suffice. The author then returns to the first interpretation's explanation: the action results in benefits that are not the motive but consequent, like shade from a tree.

Key Terms
مؤوَّل
لام العاقبة
تمثيل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The literal reading of 'that We might know' implies Allah's knowledge is temporal and acquired.
Correction: Ash'ari interpretations show the verse is metaphorical, preserving Allah's eternal knowledge.

Checkpoint
According to the fifth interpretation, to whom does the 'knowledge' in the verse actually refer?
A) To Allah
B) To the addressees
C) To the angels
D) To the Prophet

Key Takeaway
The verse 'that We might know' is interpreted metaphorically to preserve the doctrine that God's knowledge is eternal and not acquired.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you read a verse like 'that We might know', do you instinctively feel that God learns from events? What does that reveal about your heart's attachment to a literal reading, and how can you train yourself to entrust metaphor to the scholars?
?
The Ash'aris gave multiple interpretations to protect God's transcendence. Are you willing to accept that your understanding of a verse might be incomplete, even when it seems obvious? What stops you from surrendering your own interpretation?
?
If you were to explain this verse to someone who uses it to argue that God's knowledge changes, would you be able to defend the eternal nature of God's knowledge using the reasoning in this lesson? What would you need to internalize to be confident in that defense?

Following the Path of Truth and Abandoning Doubts
Sanusi exhorts to follow truth and abandon doubts after proving God's attributes.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Sanusi's exhortation to follow the path of truth and abandon doubts after establishing the necessity of God's attributes.

Core Idea
Truth is reality; doubt is uncertainty; follow the path of the people of truth.

After proving that power, will, and knowledge are necessary attributes of Allah, Sanusi turns to the reader with a direct exhortation. He defines two key concepts—truth and doubt—and calls upon the believer to embrace the way of the people of truth and discard the path of those who deny these attributes. This passage serves as both a conclusion to the discussion on attributes and a methodological guide for the seeker of sound 'aqidah.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing, then read the translation. Let the author's exhortation and definitions sink in.
The passage uses a direct command: 'فاتبع' and 'اطرح'.
2
Understand the definitions
Notice how Sanusi defines 'truth' (al-haqq) as a judgment corresponding to reality, and 'doubt' (al-riba) as an uncertainty whose correctness is unknown. These definitions are foundational.
Truth is objective; doubt is subjective uncertainty.
3
Contextualize the attributes
Recall that Sanusi has just proven the necessity of power, will, and knowledge for Allah. The exhortation builds on that knowledge.
The attributes mentioned are among the 'attributes of meanings'.
4
Identify the two groups
Sanusi contrasts the 'people of truth' (ahl al-haqq) who affirm these attributes with the 'people of doubt and deviation' (ahl al-shakk wal-zaygh) who deny them.
This implicates the Mu'tazila and others.
5
Apply the exhortation personally
Reflect on how this exhortation applies to your own study of 'aqidah: once you know the truth, follow it and leave aside doubts.
Sanusi is not just teaching information but guiding practice.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فاتبع سَبِيلَ أي : طريق الحَقِّ وهو: الحكم المطابق للواقع واطرح عنك الرِّيَب جمع رِيبَة، وهي: الشُّبهة التي لم تعلم صحتها ولا فسَادُها ، يعني : فإذا علمت وجوب القدرة ، والإرادة ، والعلم له تعالى ـ وهو سبيل أهل الحقِّ وطريقهم - فاتبعه واطرح عنكَ سبيلَ أَهلِ الشَّك والزَّيْغ النافين لها.
So follow the path – i.e., the way – of truth, and it is the judgment corresponding to reality, and discard doubts, the plural of doubt, which is an uncertainty whose correctness or corruption is not known. Meaning: when you know the necessity of power, will, and knowledge for Him, exalted – and that is the way of the people of truth and their path – then follow it and discard away from you the way of the people of doubt and deviation who deny them.
Source pages p. 253

Commentary
This passage is an exhortation. The author, after establishing the necessity of God's attributes of power, will, and knowledge (which are among the 'attributes of meanings'), calls upon the reader to follow the path of truth (al-haqq) and to abandon doubts (al-riyab). 'Truth' is defined as the judgment that corresponds to reality. 'Doubts' are defined as uncertainties (shubuhat) whose correctness or corruption is unknown. The author then clarifies the context: having known the necessity of these attributes, one is to follow the way of the people of truth and abandon the way of the people of doubt and deviation who deny these attributes. This serves as a conclusion to the section on divine attributes, reinforcing the correct position against the Mu'tazila (who denied eternal attributes) and the Karramiyya (who held attributes to be temporal).

Correction: The passage refers to all three attributes together. The exhortation to follow truth and abandon doubt applies to the whole set of necessary attributes.

After knowing the necessary attributes of Allah, one must follow the path of truth (reality) and abandon doubts.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?
The passage describes doubt as an uncertainty whose correctness is unknown. What is one uncertainty about Allah that you have been entertaining, and how will you replace it with the certainty that comes from following the path of the people of truth?

The Divine Attribute of Life: Definition and Proof
Defines divine life and proves its necessity.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the definition of God's attribute of Life, the proof for its necessity, and how it differs from created life.

Core Idea
God's life is proven by the impossibility of His other attributes without it.

This passage from Ibn Daqiq al-'Id's commentary establishes life as one of God's essential attributes. It defines divine life as an eternal quality that makes knowledge possible, and then proves why God must be living by pointing to His other perfections like knowledge, power, and will. The passage also distinguishes this eternal life from the temporary life of created beings.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Begin by reading the Arabic passage and its English translation. Notice how the text first identifies life as the fourth attribute, then defines it, provides proof, and finally contrasts it with created life.
Pay attention to the key term 'eternal attribute.'
2
Understand the definition of divine life
Focus on the phrase 'an eternal attribute that necessitates the validity of knowledge.' Reflect on why knowledge is singled out here — it shows life is foundational for intellectual attributes.
3
Grasp the proof from other attributes
The passage argues that because God has knowledge, power, will, and others, He must be living — since these cannot exist in a non-living being. See how this logical argument works.
This is a simple but powerful rational proof.
4
Contrast eternal life with originated life
Note how the text describes originated life as a quality that involves sense perception and voluntary movement. This highlights the difference between God's life and ours.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
٤ - صفة الحياة، تعريفها، ودليل وجوبها له تعالى

- حَيَاتُهُ كَذَا الْكَلَامُ السَّمْعُ ثُمَّ البَصَرْ بِذِي أَتَانَا السَّمْعُ

ورابعتُها (حَيَاتُهُ) أي : اتِّصافُ ذاتِه بالحياة (١) ، وهي : صفةٌ أزليَّة تَقتَضي صحة العِلْم .

ودليل وجوبها له تعالى وجوب اتصافه سبحانه بالعلم، والقدرة، والإرادة وغيرها (۲) ، إِذْ لا يُتصوَّر قيامها بغير حَيٍّ .

والحياة الحادثة : كيفيَّة (۳) يلزمُها قَبولُ الحس والحركة (4) الإرادية(٥).
4 – The attribute of Life, its definition, and the proof of its necessity for Him, exalted is He.

- 'His Life, likewise Speech, Hearing, then Sight, with which Hearing came to us.'

And the fourth of them (is 'His Life'), meaning: the description of His essence with life (1), and it is: an eternal attribute that necessitates the validity of knowledge.

And the proof of its necessity for Him, exalted is He, is the necessity of His being described, glorified be He, with knowledge, power, will, and others (2), since it is inconceivable for these to subsist in other than a living being.

And originated life: a quality (3) that necessitates the acceptance of sense perception and voluntary movement (4) (5).
Source pages p. 254

Commentary
This passage defines the divine attribute of Life and provides the proof for its necessity. It begins by identifying life as the fourth of the attributes mentioned in the poem, explaining that 'His Life' means the description of God's essence with life. Life is then defined as an eternal attribute that requires the validity of knowledge; in other words, it is the prerequisite for intellectual perception. The proof for God's possession of life is drawn from His other attributes: since God necessarily possesses knowledge, power, will, and other attributes, and these cannot subsist in a being that is not living, God must be living. The passage then distinguishes between eternal life (God's attribute) and originated life (created living beings). Originated life is defined as a quality that necessitates the reception of sense perception and voluntary movement, contrasting with the eternal life that is not subject to such limitations. The commentary thus establishes that life is an essential attribute of God, proven by the necessity of His other perfections.

العلم

الإرادة
الحياة الحادثة
كيفية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: God's life is similar to human life, involving sense perception and voluntary movement.
Correction: God's life is eternal and does not involve sense perception or voluntary movement, unlike originated life.

Checkpoint

Key Takeaway
God's life is an eternal attribute proven by the necessity of His other attributes (knowledge, power, will) which cannot exist without life.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

?
You may have imagined God as having human-like senses or movements. How does the contrast between divine life (which enables knowledge) and created life (which involves senses and movement) challenge that assumption? Are you ready to worship a God who is nothing like His creation?

Definition and Proof of the Attribute of Life
Sanusi defines the attribute of life and presents its proof, contrasting it with created life.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the definition of God's attribute of life, the proof for its necessity, and how it differs from created life.

Core Idea
Life is the necessary foundation for knowledge, power, and will in God.

Sanusi turns to the fourth divine attribute: life. He defines it clearly and then gives the logical proof why God must be described with it. The passage also distinguishes God's eternal life from the life of created beings, preventing confusion between the two.

Guided Path
1

Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
2
Identify the definition of God's life
Find where Sanusi defines God's life. Notice the key terms: 'eternal attribute' and 'entails soundness of knowledge.'
3
Understand the proof
The passage argues that life is necessary because other attributes like knowledge, power, and will cannot exist without a living subject. Ask yourself: Is this logically sound?
4
Note the distinction from created life
Read the description of created life: it involves sense perception and voluntary movement. Contrast this with God's life which is eternal and not subject to these.
5

ورابعتُها (حَيَاتُهُ) أي : اتِّصافُ ذاتِه بالحياة (١) ، وهي : صفةٌ أزليَّة تَقتَضي صحة العِلْم . ودليل وجوبها له تعالى وجوب اتصافه سبحانه بالعلم، والقدرة، والإرادة وغيرها (۲) ، إِذْ لا يُتصوَّر قيامها بغير حَيٍّ . والحياة الحادثة : كيفيَّة (۳) يلزمُها قَبُولُ الحس والحركة (4) الإرادية(٥).

Source pages p. 254

This passage defines the attribute of life and gives the proof for its necessity for God. The definition is given: an eternal attribute that entails the soundness of knowledge. Finally, it defines created life as a quality that necessarily requires receiving sense perception and voluntary movement. This distinguishes God's eternal life from contingent life.

The Meaning of Life: Sanusi's Definition and Its Rational Condition
Sanusi defines life and explains its rational condition.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Sanusi's definition of life, its components as genus and differentia, and how it functions as a rational condition for perception.

Core Idea
Life makes perception possible without making it necessary.

Sanusi begins the discussion on life by providing a precise definition that applies to both God's eternal life and the created life of contingent beings. The definition is built from two key parts: the genus 'attribute' and the differentia 'renders correct being described with attributes of perception.' Understanding these components clarifies exactly what life is and what it does.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully.
Read the Arabic passage once, then the translation. Notice the structure: the author lists three aspects but focuses on the first.
The definition is the core.
2
Identify the genus and differentia.
The definition has two parts: 'attribute' (genus) and 'renders correct ...' (differentia). See how these work to include all life and exclude other attributes.
Genus includes all attributes; differentia specifies life.
3
Understand 'renders correct' as possibility.
The commentator explains that 'renders correct' means 'makes possible' – life does not cause perception but allows it. This is the rational condition.
Life is not a cause but a condition.
4
Apply to God and creatures.
For God, what is possible becomes necessary due to His perfection; for creatures, life makes perception possible but not guaranteed (e.g., sleep).
This shows the definition covers both types of life.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الكلام في صفة الحياة يتعلق بها من ثلاثة أوجه الأول: بيان معناها، الثاني: ذكر الدليل اتصاف الله تعالى بصفة الحياة، والثالث بيان هل تتعلق الحياة بشيء ؟ أمَّا الكلام على الأوَّل فَنقولُ : عرَّف الشَّيخ السنوسي الحياة بتعريف يشمل الحياة القديمة والحياة الحادثة حيث قال: (وهي صفة تُصرِّحُ لِمَن قامت به أن يتصف بصفات الإدراك» فقوله (صفة) كالجنس في التعريف يشمل جميع الصفات وقوله: تصحح ... إلخ» كالفصل أخرج جميع الصفات غير المعرّف ومعنى: «تصحح» تُجوّز ، فالحياة شرط عقلي للاتصاف بصفات الإدراك ، ومعنى كونها شرطًا عقليّا أَنَّه يلزم من عدمها عدم الإدراك ، ولا يلزم من وجودها وجود الإدراك ولا عدمه، والمراد من تجويزها ذلك عدم الاستحالة ، والمعنى على هذا: أنَّ عند وجود الحياة لا يستحيل الاتصاف بصفات الإدراك ، والاتصاف بصفات الإدراك عند وجود الحياة ممكن بالإمكان العام الذي يشمل الواجب والمُستوي الطرفين، وحينئذ يجعل الاتصاف بالإدراك عند وجود الحياة بالنسبة إلى الحوادث مستوي الطرفين، وبالنسبة إلى القديم سبحانه واجبا، والحاصل : أنَّ كلمة «تصحح» الواقعة في هذا التعريف معناها بالنظر إلى القديم توجب له سبحانه أن يتصف بالإدراك أزلاً وأبدا؛ لأن كل ما صح في حقه تعالى فهو واجب ، ومعناها بالنظر إلى الحوادث : تُجوِّز أن يتصف بالإدراك، ألا تری حالة أنا قد نكون في نوم أو غفلة ؟ فيكون الإدراك غير موجود وإن كانت الحياة موجودة. وإِنَّما قال: «تصحح لمن قامت به أن يتصف بصفات الإدراك» دون أن يقول: «تصحح لمن قَامَتْ به أَنْ يُدرِكَ» ؛ لأنَّ الذي من لوازم الحياة صحة الإدراك لا الإدراك نفسه، والإدراك ههنا يشمل : العلم ، والسمع، والبصر، وغيرها. فإن قلت : فالحياة شرط للاتصاف بغير الإدراك من الصفات، كالقدرة، والإرادة، والكلام، فإنَّ كلَّ واحدة من هذه الصفات يستحيل وجودها بغير الحياة، فلماذا اقتصر على ذكر الإدراك ؟ قلتُ: إِنَّ كون الحياة شرطًا للاتصاف بغير الإدراك من الصفات يُفهم بطريق اللزوم، وذلكَ لأنَّ القُدرة والإرادة والكلام وهذه صفات ملزومة للإدراك ، وكل ما كان شرطًا في حصول اللازم فهو البَتَّةَ شرط في حصول الملزوم، فاقتصاره على ذكر الإدراك ؛ لكونه يَستَتبع ما ذكرتَ مِن الصِّفات، وبعبارة أخرى : إنَّ القدرة والإرادة وغيرهما من الصفات لا تتحقق إلَّا بالعلم، فالعلم شرطٌ فيها ، وجعل الحياة شرطًا في الاتصاف بالعلم، يستدعي كونها شرطًاً في الاتصاف بالصِّفاتِ الأخرى ، لأنَّ شرط الشرط شرط . ومن العلماء من عرَّف الحياة القديمة التي هي صفة الله تعالى بتعريف ، وعرّف الحياة الحادثة صفة للحوادث بتعريف آخر ، فعرّف الحياة القديمة بأنها: «صفةٌ أزليَّة تقتضي العلم» ، أي: تقتضي صحة الاتّصاف بالعلم، وكما تقتضي صحة الاتصاف بالعلم تقتضي صحة الاتصاف بغيره من الصفات الواجبة له تعالى ، وقد عرفت وجه ذلك كله فيما تقدَّم، وعرف الحياة الحادثة بأنها: «كيفيَّةٌ يَلزَمُها قَبُولُ الحِس والحركة الإرادية» ، وقد سلك الشارح التي هي صحة هذا المسلك
The discussion about the attribute of life relates to it from three aspects: first, explaining its meaning; second, mentioning the proof of God's being described with the attribute of life; third, explaining whether life is related to something. As for the discussion on the first, we say: Sheikh Sanusi defined life with a definition that includes eternal life and created life. He said: 'It is an attribute that renders correct for the one in whom it subsists to be described with attributes of perception.' So his saying 'attribute' is like the genus in the definition, including all attributes, and his saying 'renders correct' ... etc. is like the differentia, excluding all attributes other than the defined one. And the meaning of 'renders correct' is 'makes possible'. So life is a rational condition for being described with attributes of perception. And the meaning of it being a rational condition is that from its absence the absence of perception is necessary, but from its existence neither the existence nor the absence of perception is necessary. And what is meant by 'makes possible' is the absence of impossibility. And the meaning based on this is that when life exists, it is not impossible to be described with attributes of perception, and being described with attributes of perception when life exists is possible with the general possibility that includes the necessary and the two equally possible sides. Then at that point, being described with perception when life exists is made equally possible with respect to contingent beings, and necessary with respect to the Eternal, exalted. And the upshot: that the word 'renders correct' occurring in this definition, with respect to the Eternal, necessitates for Him, exalted, that He be described with perception eternally and forever, because everything that is valid (sahha) regarding Him, exalted, is necessary. And its meaning with respect to contingents: it makes possible being described with perception. Do you not see how we may be in sleep or heedlessness? Then perception is non-existent even though life is existent. And he only said 'renders correct for the one in whom it subsists to be described with attributes of perception' rather than saying 'renders correct for the one in whom it subsists to perceive', because what is entailed by life is the soundness of perception, not perception itself. And perception here includes knowledge, hearing, sight, and others. If you say: 'Life is a condition for being described with other attributes besides perception, such as power, will, and speech, for each of these attributes cannot exist except with life, so why did he restrict mention to perception?' I say: 'Indeed, life being a condition for being described with other than perception among attributes is understood by way of implication. That is because power, will, and speech are attributes that are implied (malzum) by perception, and everything that is a condition for obtaining the necessary (lazim) is definitely a condition for obtaining the implied (malzum). So he restricted mention to perception because it entails what you mentioned of attributes. In other words, power, will, and others are not realized except through knowledge, so knowledge is a condition for them, and making life a condition for being described with knowledge necessitates it being a condition for being described with the other attributes, because the condition of a condition is a condition.' And among scholars are those who defined eternal life, which is an attribute of God, with a definition, and defined created life, an attribute of contingents, with another definition. So they defined eternal life as: 'An eternal attribute that necessitates knowledge,' meaning it necessitates the soundness of being described with knowledge, and just as it necessitates the soundness of being described with knowledge, it necessitates the soundness of being described with other attributes necessary for Him, exalted. And you have known the face of all that in what preceded. And they defined created life as: 'A quality that necessarily requires the reception of sense perception and voluntary movement.' And the commentator has followed this path.
Source pages p. 254

Commentary
This passage is a detailed commentary on the definition of life. It first notes three aspects: meaning, proof, and relation. Focusing on the first, it quotes Sanusi's definition: 'an attribute that renders correct for the one in whom it subsists to be described with attributes of perception.' The commentator explains that 'attribute' is the genus and 'renders correct' is the differentia, meaning 'makes possible'. Life is a rational condition for perception: its absence necessitates absence of perception, but its existence does not necessitate perception itself (e.g., dreaming). Concerning God, whatever is valid becomes necessary, so life necessitates perception for Him. The commentator then answers an objection: why mention only perception when life is also a condition for power, will, and speech? The answer uses the principle of implication: these attributes are implied by perception (via knowledge), so life being a condition for perception extends to them through the condition of the condition. Finally, alternative definitions are given: eternal life as an eternal attribute necessitating knowledge (which implies necessity of other attributes), and created life as a quality requiring sense perception and voluntary movement. The commentator notes this is the path followed.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think life directly causes perception.
Correction: Life only makes perception possible; it does not necessitate it. The text shows that in sleep, life exists without perception.

Key Takeaway
Life is a rational condition that makes perception possible but not necessary; for God, it necessitates perception due to His perfection.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You have life right now, but are you perceiving God's signs, or are you in a state of spiritual heedlessness like sleep? What can you do today to turn your life into active perception?
?
If life is only a condition for perception, what does your current lack of awareness or forgetfulness reveal about your relationship with your own existence? Will you remain in that state, or will you strive to make your life a means of knowing God?
?
God's life necessitates His perfect perception of you at every moment. How does this reality confront your hidden actions and thoughts? What will you change today because you know you are always seen?

Why Only Perception? Implication and Two Definitions of Life
Objection about the scope of life's conditionality and alternative definitions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Sanusi's definition only mentions perception despite life being a condition for other attributes, and how eternal and created life are defined separately.

Core Idea
Life is a condition for other attributes because it is a condition for knowledge, which is a condition for them.

After presenting the definition of life, the commentator addresses a natural objection: if life is required for power, will, and speech, why does the definition only mention perception? The answer uses the principle of implication (luzum) and the rule that the condition of a condition is itself a condition. Then the commentator presents alternative definitions that distinguish between God's eternal life and the created life of contingent beings.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection and answer.
Read the passage where the hypothetical questioner raises the objection and the commentator responds. Notice the logical structure: the objection is based on the fact that life is a condition for other attributes too.
The answer uses the concept of implication (luzum) between attributes.
2
Understand the implication chain.
Power, will, and speech are implied (malzum) by perception (lazim). Life is a condition for perception, so it becomes a condition for these other attributes indirectly.
Also, knowledge is a condition for these attributes, and life is a condition for knowledge.
3
Study the alternative definitions.
Some scholars define eternal life as an eternal attribute that necessitates knowledge (and through it, other attributes), and created life as a quality requiring sense perception and voluntary movement.
These definitions are more specific to each type of life.

توجب له سبحانه أن يتصف بالإدراك أزلاً وأبدا؛ لأن كل ما صح في حقه تعالى فهو واجب ، ومعناها بالنظر إلى الحوادث : تُجوِّز أن يتصف بالإدراك، ألا تری حالة أنا قد نكون في نوم أو غفلة ؟ فيكون الإدراك غير ههنا يشمل : العلم ، والسمع، والبصر، وغيرها. فإن قلت : فالحياة شرط للاتصاف بغير الإدراك من الصفات، كالقدرة، والإرادة، والكلام، فإنَّ كلَّ واحدة من هذه الصفات يستحيل وجودها بغير الحياة، فلماذا اقتصر على The discussion about the attribute of life relates to it from three aspects: first, explaining its meaning; second, mentioning the proof of God's being described with the attribute of life; third, explaining whether life is related to something. As for the discussion on the first, we say: Sheikh Sanusi defined life with a definition that includes eternal life and created life. He said: 'It is an attribute that renders correct for the one in whom it subsists to be described with attributes of perception.' So his saying 'attribute' is like the genus in the definition, including all attributes, and his saying 'renders correct' ... etc. is like the differentia, excluding all attributes other than the defined one. And the meaning of 'renders correct' is 'makes possible'. So life is a rational condition for being described with attributes of perception. And the meaning of it being a rational condition is that from its absence the absence of perception is necessary, but from its existence neither the existence nor the absence of perception is necessary. And what is meant by 'makes possible' is the absence of impossibility. And the meaning based on this is that when life exists, it is not impossible to be described with attributes of perception, and being described with attributes of perception when life exists is possible with the general possibility that includes the necessary and the two equally possible sides. Then at that point, being described with perception when life exists is made equally possible with respect to contingent beings, and necessary with respect to the Eternal, exalted. And its meaning with respect to contingents: it makes possible being described with perception. Do you not see how we may be in sleep or heedlessness? Then perception is non-existent even though life is existent. And he only said 'renders correct for the one in whom it subsists to be described with attributes of perception' rather than saying 'renders correct for the one in whom it subsists to perceive', because what is entailed by life is the soundness of perception, not perception itself. And perception here includes knowledge, hearing, sight, and others. So he restricted mention to perception because it entails what you mentioned of attributes. In other words, power, will, and others are not realized except through knowledge, so knowledge is a condition for them, and making life a condition for being described with knowledge necessitates it being a condition for being described with the other attributes, because the condition of a condition is a condition.' Divine Attributes

Definition of Life and the Meaning of 'Tusahhih'
Definition of life and explanation of 'tusahhih'.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the definition of the divine attribute of life and the precise meaning of the word 'tusahhih' (makes correct) as it applies to the Eternal and to originated beings.

Core Idea
Life does not cause perception; it makes perception correct and possible.

The author begins by defining life as an attribute that makes correct the description with perception. He carefully explains that 'tusahhih' means different things for God and for creatures: for God, it necessitates actual perception; for creatures, it merely permits it. This lesson unpacks that crucial distinction.

Guided Path
1
Read the definition
Read the Arabic definition of life and its translation. Notice the key term 'tusahhih' (makes correct). Ask yourself what it means for something to 'make correct' an attribute.
The definition is precise: 'an attribute that makes correct for the one in whom it subsists to be described with attributes of perception.'
2
Understand 'tusahhih' for the Eternal
For Allah, whatever is correct is necessary. So 'tusahhih' means that life necessitates actual perception eternally. Understand the reasoning: if something is correct (not impossible) for Allah, it becomes obligatory due to His perfection.
This is a key principle in kalam: for Allah, possibility leads to necessity.
3
Understand 'tusahhih' for creatures
For creatures, 'tusahhih' means permission or removal of impossibility. Life makes perception possible, but not mandatory. The example of sleep shows that life can exist without actual perception.
Contrast this with the Eternal case: for us, life only makes perception possible.
4
Note why 'attributes of perception' not 'perception'
The author explicitly says life entails the correctness of perception (sihha), not perception itself. Perception itself is the actual act. This nuance is important to avoid misunderstanding.
Perception includes knowledge, hearing, sight, etc.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قسم الإلهيات: صفة تصحح لمن قامت به أن يتصف بصفات الإدراك. وجودها وجود الإدراك ولا عدمه، والمراد من تجويزها ذلك عدم الاستحالة، والمعنى على هذا: أنَّ عند وجود الحياة لا يستحيل الاتصاف بصفات الإدراك، والاتصاف بصفات الإدراك عند وجود الحياة ممكن بالإمكان العام الذي يشمل الواجب والمُستوي الطرفين، وحينئذ يجعل الاتصاف بالإدراك عند وجود الحياة بالنسبة إلى الحوادث مستوي الطرفين، وبالنسبة إلى القديم سبحانه واجبا، والحاصل: أنَّ كلمة «تصحح» الواقعة في هذا التعريف معناها بالنظر إلى القديم توجب له سبحانه أن يتصف بالإدراك أزلاً وأبدا؛ لأن كل ما صح في حقه تعالى فهو واجب، ومعناها بالنظر إلى الحوادث: تُجوِّز أن يتصف بالإدراك، ألا ترى حالة أنا قد نكون في نوم أو غفلة؟ فيكون الإدراك غير موجود وإن كانت الحياة موجودة. وإِنَّما قال: «تصحح لمن قامت به أن يتصف بصفات الإدراك» دون أن يقول: «تصحح لمن قامت به أن يدرك»؛ لأنَّ الذي من لوازم الحياة صحة الإدراك لا الإدراك نفسه، والإدراك ههنا يشمل: العلم، والسمع، والبصر، وغيرها. فإن قلت: فالحياة شرط للاتصاف بغير الإدراك من الصفات، كالقدرة، والإرادة، والكلام، فإنَّ كلَّ واحدة من هذه الصفات يستحيل وجودها بغير الحياة، فلماذا اقتصر على ذكر الإدراك؟ قلت: إِنَّ كون الحياة شرطًا للاتصاف بغير الإدراك من الصفات يُفهم بطريق اللزوم، وذلكَ لأنَّ القُدرة والإرادة والكلام وهذه صفات ملزومة للإدراك، وكل ما كان شرطًا في حصول اللازم فهو البَتَّةَ شرط في حصول الملزوم، فاقتصاره على ذكر الإدراك؛ لكونه يَستَتبع ما ذكرتَ مِن الصِّفات، وبعبارة أخرى: إنَّ القدرة والإرادة وغيرهما من الصفات لا تتحقق إلَّا بالعلم، فالعلم شرطٌ فيها، وجعل الحياة شرطًا في الاتصاف بالعلم، يستدعي كونها شرطًا في الاتصاف بالصِّفاتِ الأخرى، لأنَّ شرط الشرط شرط. ومن العلماء من عرَّف الحياة القديمة التي هي صفة الله تعالى بتعريف، وعرّف الحياة الحادثة صفة للحوادث بتعريف آخر، فعرّف الحياة القديمة بأنها: «صفةٌ أزليَّة تقتضي العلم»، أي: تقتضي صحة الاتّصاف بالعلم، وكما تقتضي صحة الاتصاف بالعلم تقتضي صحة الاتصاف بغيره من الصفات الواجبة له تعالى، وقد عرفت وجه ذلك كله فيما تقدَّم، وعرف الحياة الحادثة بأنها: «كيفيَّةٌ يَلزَمُها قَبُولُ الحِس والحركة الإرادية»، وقد سلك الشارح صحة هذا المسلك.
The Section on Divine Matters: An attribute that makes correct for the one in whom it subsists to be described with attributes of perception. Its existence is the existence of perception and not its non-existence, and the intended meaning of its making permissible is the absence of impossibility. The meaning based on this is that when life exists, being described with attributes of perception is not impossible, and being described with attributes of perception when life exists is possible with the general possibility that includes the obligatory and the two-sided equal. At that point, being described with perception when life exists is made two-sided equal with respect to originated things, and with respect to the Eternal, Exalted is He, obligatory. The conclusion is that the word 'makes correct' occurring in this definition, its meaning with respect to the Eternal necessitates for Him, Exalted is He, to be described with perception eternally and forever, because everything that is correct in His right, Exalted is He, is obligatory. Its meaning with respect to originated things is that it makes permissible that they be described with perception. Do you not see the case where we may be in sleep or heedlessness? Perception is non-existent even though life exists. He only said: 'makes correct for the one in whom it subsists to be described with attributes of perception' and did not say 'makes correct for the one in whom it subsists to perceive', because that which is entailed by life is the correctness of perception, not perception itself. Perception here includes: knowledge, hearing, sight, and others. If you say: Life is a condition for being described with other than perception from the attributes, such as power, will, and speech, for each of these attributes is impossible in existence without life, so why did he limit to mentioning perception? I answer: Indeed, life being a condition for being described with other than perception from the attributes is understood by way of entailment, because power, will, and speech are attributes that entail perception, and whatever is a condition for obtaining the entailed thing is absolutely a condition for obtaining the entailing thing. So his restriction to mentioning perception is because it entails those attributes you mentioned. In other words, power, will, and others are not realized except through knowledge, so knowledge is a condition for them. And making life a condition for being described with knowledge necessitates it being a condition for being described with the other attributes, because the condition of a condition is a condition. Some scholars defined the eternal life which is the attribute of Allah Most High with one definition, and defined the temporal life as an attribute of originated things with another definition. They defined eternal life as: 'An eternal attribute that necessitates knowledge,' i.e., it necessitates the correctness of being described with knowledge, and just as it necessitates the correctness of being described with knowledge, it necessitates the correctness of being described with other than it of the attributes obligatory for Him Most High. You have known the face of all that in what preceded. And they defined temporal life as: 'A quality which necessitates the reception of sense and voluntary motion.' And the commentator has followed this path.
Source pages p. 255

Commentary
This passage defines the divine attribute of life and explains its meaning. It begins with the definition: life is an attribute that makes correct for the one in whom it subsists to be described with attributes of perception. The author then unpacks the word 'tusahhih' (makes correct): for God, whatever is correct is necessary, so life entails actual perception eternally; for creatures, it merely permits perception, as seen in sleep where life exists but perception does not. The definition says 'attributes of perception' rather than 'perception' because life entails the correctness (sihha) of perception, not perception itself. Perception includes knowledge, hearing, sight, etc. An objection is raised: why mention only perception when life is also a condition for attributes like power, will, and speech? The answer is that these attributes actually entail perception, so life being a condition for perception makes it a condition for them by entailment (luzum). Alternatively, power and will require knowledge, and knowledge is a type of perception; thus condition of condition applies. Finally, the passage presents alternative definitions distinguishing eternal life (an eternal attribute necessitating knowledge) and temporal life (a quality necessitating sense reception and voluntary motion), noting that the commentator followed this path.

Key Terms
الحياة
تصحح
الإدراك

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That life itself is the perception or that life directly causes perception.
Correction: Life is only the condition for the correctness of perception; it does not cause perception. For creatures, life can exist without perception (e.g., sleep).

Checkpoint
Why does the definition of life mention 'attributes of perception' rather than simply 'perception'?
A) Because life directly causes perception
B) Because life entails the correctness of perception, not perception itself
C) Because perception is impossible without life
D) Because the author wanted to be brief

Key Takeaway
Life is the condition for the correctness of perception, not perception itself, and its effect differs for the Eternal and for creatures.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You are alive right now, but are you truly perceiving Allah's signs and attributes? Or are you like the sleeping person—alive but unaware? What specific ayat or creation will you contemplate today to actively perceive your Lord?
?
The passage says that for Allah, life necessitates eternal and perfect perception. How does that truth expose your own limitations? In what area of your life have you been relying on your own existence to give you knowledge, rather than seeking it actively from Allah?
?
If life only makes perception possible for you, not guaranteed, then what are you doing to ensure you are not in a state of 'heedlessness'? Commit to one concrete action this week to wake your heart to the reality of Allah's constant perception.
